### Set Col

#### Indigenous organizations oppose geoengineering research because it's a distraction from emission cuts

Aslak-Antti **Oksanen** 20**23**, (Senior Lecturer at the University of
Bristol), Frontiers in Climate, 20 Feb 2023, "Dimming the midnight sun?
Implications of the Sámi Council\'s intervention against the SCoPEx
project" https://doi.org/10.3389/fclim.2023.994193

From a humanitarian perspective, indigenous peoples are amongst those
most vulnerable to climate change. The impacts of climate change on
indigenous peoples are global but take different forms in different
regions, ranging from pacific islanders risking displacement by rising
sea levels (Voyatzis-Bouillard and Kelman, 2021), to pastoralists in the
Sahel suffering the consequences of droughts and land (Dieng, 2021) and
the myriad negative effects unpredictable weather conditions have on
people living close to nature (Jones, 2019; Schramm et al., 2020). The
Sámi people, who inhabit northern Fennoscandia and the Russian Kola
peninsula have also experienced adverse effects of climate change, with
mid-winter thaws resulting in icy snows blocking access to reindeer
pastures, forcing Sámi reindeer herders to resort to costly emergency
fodder, and unpredictable weather conditions complicating seasonal herd
migrations (personal correspondence with Sámi reindeer herders).
[[Geoengineering advocates]{.mark} could therefore [expect]{.mark}
[that]{.mark} the Sámi, along with other [indigenous peoples]{.mark},
[may be in favor of geoengineering research]{.mark} that could generate
knowledge with the potential for drastically mitigating or even
reversing global warming. [However, there has been declared opposition
from indigenous organizations through statements like the Anchorage
Declaration]{.mark}. [The Sámi have recently joined ranks]{.mark} [with
indigenous peoples opposing geoengineering research through the Sámi
Council\'s open letters demanding the cancellation of the planned test
flight for Harvard University\'s SCoPEx
project]{.mark}]{.underline}[,]{.mark} which had been scheduled for June
2021 on Sámi domicile lands in Giron/Kiruna (northern Sweden) and the
halting of the project altogether. The SCoPEx project aims to explore
some of the potential side-effects of Stratospheric Aerosol Injection
(SAI) as a way of cooling the planet\'s temperature by reflecting more
sunlight (Low et al., 2022, p. 5). [[Rather than embracing]{.mark} the
concept of [geoengineering]{.mark} as a potential solution for averting
climate disaster, [most indigenous organizations engaged in the question
have rejected it as a distraction from measures necessary to curb
climate change, like emission cuts]{.mark}]{.underline} (The Anchorage
Declaration, 2009).

#### Empirically Geoengineering research projects exclude indigenous input

Aslak-Antti **Oksanen** 20**23**, (Senior Lecturer at the University of
Bristol), Frontiers in Climate, 20 Feb 2023, "Dimming the midnight sun?
Implications of the Sámi Council\'s intervention against the SCoPEx
project" https://doi.org/10.3389/fclim.2023.994193

From the webinar and the other publicly available information, we can
see that there was coordination between the Sámi Council and HOME member
organizations---specifically the Indigenous Environmental Network, the
ETC Group, and the Heinrich Böll Foundation---in their opposition to the
SCoPEx project\'s planned Giron/Kiruna test flight. The Sámi Council\'s
open letter,1 dated 24.02.2021, addressed to the SSC, the Swedish
Government and the SCoPEx Advisory Committee was preceded by a letter
sent weeks earlier to the SSC and the Swedish government, co-signed by
three aforementioned organizations, Greenpeace Sweden, Friends of the
Earth Sweden, Friends of the Earth International and others. [There are
clear similarities between the [letters]{.mark} as both [highlights the
unpredictability of SAI technology with potential for extreme
consequences]{.mark}, the danger of [a termination shock]{.mark}, of it
[deflating pressure to transition to carbon neutrality]{.mark}, and the
[lack of]{.mark} both Swedish [representation and consultation with
affected indigenous populations]{.mark} as required by
UNDRIP]{.underline} (2007) (Open Letter to the SSC and Swedish
Government by Greenpeace Sweden, Jordens Vänner/Friends of the Earth
Sweden, Naturskyddsföreningen/Swedish Society for Nature Conservation
(SSNC), Action Group on Erosion, Technology and Concentration (ETC
Group), Biofuelwatch, Center for International Environmental Law (CIEL),
Climate Justice Alliance (CJA), Friend of the Earth International,
Heinrich Böll Foundation, Indigenous Environmental Network (IEN),
WhatNext? (2020); Open Letter to the SCoPEx Advisory Committee by the
Sámi Council Open Letter to the SCoPEx Advisory Committee (2021). [[The
Sámi Council\'s letter also raises]{.mark} further [objections toward
**the homogenous composition of the**]{.mark} **SCoPEx [Advisory
Committee]{.mark}**, [consisting almost entirely of US citizens]{.mark}
and/or residents, [and its approach to research ethics]{.mark} as
expressed in the draft "Engagement Process for SCoPEx," [which states
that no research project should have to answer questions pertaining to
the potential moral hazard of geoengineering deployment and surrounding
decision making and **governance issues**]{.mark}. [The letter
argues]{.mark} "that precisely because of the extraordinary and
particular risks associated with [SAI]{.mark}, this technology and
SCoPEx **[cannot be treated like other research]{.mark}**." The letter
concludes by stating that [research]{.mark} along the lines of SCoPEx
[cannot be carried out before concerns]{.mark} such as those raised in
the letter [have been addressed]{.mark} and full global consensus on its
acceptability has been reached.]{.underline}

The close partnership that formed between the HOME coalition,
represented by the ETC Group, Heinrich Böll Foundation, and the
Indigenous Environmental Network, and the Sámi Council in articulating
their opposition to the aims of the SCoPEx project can be further
discerned from the webinar co-hosted by these organizations live
streamed through the ETC Group\'s Facebook page on June 6th, 2021 (ETC
Group\'s Facebook Page, 2021). The normative influence of the Sámi
Council\'s intervention against the SCoPEx project can be seen from how
the webinar drew the participation of the global influencers in the
climate change debate of Naomi Klein, Vandana Shiva, Bill McKibben and
Greta Thunberg in solidarity with the Sámi people. The rhetoric of the
webinar contributors shows the discursive nodal points that formed the
chains of equivalency within this diverse group and how this was posited
in antagonistic opposition to what the SCoPEx project was seen to
represent.

Vice President, Åsa Larsson Blind represented the Sámi Council at the
webinar[. In her opening statement, she said that [the Sámi Council had
been unaware of the SCoPEx project and the larger question of
geoengineering]{.mark}, and was alerted to SCoPEx\'s planned activities
in Giron/Kiruna in February 2021 by Tom Goldtooth of the Indigenous
Environmental Network and the ETC Group]{.underline}. This triggered
what Larsson Blind described as an "instinctive" response by the Sámi
Council as expressed in the open letter:

#### Geoengineering is the same worldview of master over nature that got us into the climate crisis in the first place -- Turns case

Aslak-Antti **Oksanen** 20**23**, (Senior Lecturer at the University of
Bristol), Frontiers in Climate, 20 Feb 2023, "Dimming the midnight sun?
Implications of the Sámi Council\'s intervention against the SCoPEx
project" https://doi.org/10.3389/fclim.2023.994193

"We reacted quite instinctively when we learned what the idea of solar
geoengineering is, and what they are attempting to do. [We reacted
instinctively, because this goes against our world view, that we as
humans should live and adapt to nature, and [it is against the respect
with which we are taught to treat nature and mother earth]{.mark}. [And
it is the whole]{.mark} idea that the [line of thought that has put us
in the climate crisis in the first place]{.mark} should also be the one
thing taking us out of it. And there our position is clear, that [now is
the time for all society to listen to what mother earth is telling
us]{.mark} and nature is telling us now. Our focus should be on finding
a way to a sustainable climate-friendly society]{.underline}. And this
is why we have reacted... together with other organizations and allies,
with the result of the cancellation of the test in Giron."

In Blind\'s statement, we can see the first discursive nodal point, [of
[a world view of living in harmony with mother earth]{.mark}, which [is
posited in opposition to **technological manipulation** of nature
through]{.mark} solar [geoengineering]{.mark}.]{.underline}

Blind went on to state a quite categorical opposition to solar
geoengineering, and that the Sámi Council was working to broaden
indigenous opposition to it:

"But now that we have engaged ourselves in the issue, our position is
clear, that this technology of solar geoengineering, that is not a part
of our chosen future. So, this is also why we have taken the initiative
to gather indigenous peoples from around the world in a joint letter to
Harvard University, urging them to put a stop to the SCoPEx project.
[And the message [in this letter]{.mark}, is that [we as indigenous
peoples do not approve of the legitimizing development toward]{.mark}
solar [geoengineering]{.mark} [and nor for it to be conducted in or
above our lands or territories and skies, nor in any ecosystem
anywhere]{.mark}."]{.underline}

The central discursive nodal point of the opposition between the
extractive, mechanistic mastery over nature mentality and its
technological solutions to climate change, and respect for the sanctity
of Mother Earth and nature-based solutions also came through strongly in
Vandana Shiva\'s contribution. Shiva is a physicist who founded the
Research Foundation for Science, Technology, and Natural Resource Policy
(RFSTN), author of numerous books, including Ecofeminism, Gates to a
Global Empire and We Feed the World, and a social and environmental
activist. [Her contribution began with stressing how the idea of [SAI
was expressive of the mistaken assumption that control and mastery over
nature is possible and desirable]{.mark}:]{.underline}

["You are [adding pollution to the existing pollution]{.mark}, creating
the same instability. Pollution for a pollution problem; [deliberate
climate change as a solution to climate change is]{.mark} not just
[insane]{.mark}, but [it is]{.mark}, as Einstein said, [repeating the
mindset that got you into the crisis in the first place]{.mark}. And
what is that mindset? [A mechanistic worldview. The idea of mastery and
control, of engineering a solution for everything
living]{.mark}."]{.underline}

#### Pro-geoengineering "science" is fraught with [conflict of interests]{.underline} from [capitalists]{.underline} who benefit from backing a ["techno-fix"]{.underline}

[[Naomi Klein]{.mark}, author of numerous influential books like No

#### Geoengineering reproduces colonial terra nullius logic when they find "empty" places to experiment

"So, lack of consultation is a big concern with our indigenous peoples.

the tool of the UN Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples, our
indigenous peoples, in every region we reside, including Sweden, have a

#### Geoengineering research is funded by the rich and the positive results will only go to the rich -- excluding the global south and indigenous people

Aslak-Antti **Oksanen** 20**23**, (Senior Lecturer at the University of
Bristol), Frontiers in Climate, 20 Feb 2023, "Dimming the midnight sun?

Bill McKibben, author of books like The End of Nature and Falter, and

[geoengineering]{.mark} being [a preoccupation of billionaires
benefitting from the extractivist status quo]{.mark}. He
[divided]{.mark} the [billionaires into two camps]{.mark}: [those who

[the]{.mark} global [economy]{.mark}:**]{.underline}

["[Bill Gates]{.mark}, who [did the original funding for much of this
research]{.mark}, is of the stay at home and come up with some

which is to get off the coal and gas and oil and replace it with sun and

that we have to move fast, that Jeff Bezos aside, there is no planet B,
and we are not going to someplace else]{.mark}.... This is really an
incredibly beautiful planet, the only one that we know of that supports

we know works."]{.underline}

#### Indigenous people must be a part of a sustainable solution and geoengineering isn't compatible with that equation

Aslak-Antti **Oksanen** 20**23**, (Senior Lecturer at the University of
Bristol), Frontiers in Climate, 20 Feb 2023, "Dimming the midnight sun?
Implications of the Sámi Council\'s intervention against the SCoPEx
project" https://doi.org/10.3389/fclim.2023.994193

Åsa Larsson Blind\'s statement in the questions and answers part shows
the potential long term implications of the SCoPEx project\'s planned
test flight in Giron/Kiruna on indigenous peoples\' standing in relation

relation to the SCoPEx project, she stated that the Sámi Council had
written an open letter to Harvard University for which they were
collecting co-signatures from indigenous peoples\' organizations from
around the world before sending it:

Through the letter "we hope that we can show that we have a joint
position and that we actually can influence the discussion. And to focus
on what we should discuss. And I would like to also touch upon what Tom
\[Goldtooth\] said, because I believe that it is very important. [I\'m
convinced that [indigenous peoples\' knowledge has the potential to play
a significant role and a way toward a sustainable future for
all]{.mark}.... So, this step that we are taking, is also to that we, as
indigenous peoples, [we want to contribute to the solutions]{.mark}.
[And we do not see]{.mark} solar [geoengineering as part of
that]{.mark}. So, we hope that our letter can help us get into the
discussion."]{.underline}

[We can see from the below statement from Blind that the [SCoPEx
project]{.mark} team, through its conduct in failing to consult the Sámi

an effective antagonistic reference point in relation to which a global
indigenous coalition opposed to geoengineering could be

["We have taken this initiative, but it is on behalf of indigenous
voices everywhere. We see the response that we get to this letter, that
[we have major indigenous organizations from all over the world]{.mark}.

[[The]{.mark} open [letter]{.mark} addressed at Harvard University,
dated 04.06.2021, [was co-signed by more than 30 indigenous peoples\'
organizations from around the world]{.mark}]{.underline} (Doyle, 2021;
Sámi Council Open Letter to Harvard University, 2021). This suggests
that the Sámi Council\'s global leadership among indigenous peoples has
potential for significant impact in shaping their attitudes toward
geoengineering research and in expanding the indigenous contingent of
the anti-geoengineering coalition of indigenous peoples\' organizations
and environmental civil society groups. The tone adopted in the letter
was a bit different from that of the Sámi Council\'s previous letter to
the SCC, as it did not focus on any of the specifics of SCoPEx planned
activities in Giron/Kiruna, but rather, centered on the value-driven,
core discursive nodal point of antagonism between living in harmony with
Mother Nature, and the mastery over nature mentality expressed though
the concept of geoengineering:

"We note with greatest concern that efforts toward the potential
development of solar geoengineering technology are now taken and planned
to be performed through field tests. [[SAI]{.mark} (stratospheric
aerosol injection) technology builds upon artificially manipulating the
environment and thereafter, if deployed, might [generate irreversible
changes to natural systems.]{.mark}]{.underline} [[Climate manipulation
strongly contradicts our understanding and experience of how to respect
and live in harmony with Mother Nature, and therefore, this technology
is not something we see as a part of our chosen
future]{.mark}."]{.underline}

[From the above, we can see how [the SCoPEx project provided a reference
point for the deepening of a pre-existing alliance of green civil
society organizations and indigenous peoples organizations centered on
the HOME campaign]{.mark}, with the first step consisting of the Sámi
Council opting to join it, followed by its effort to mobilize indigenous
peoples from around the world, with some apparent success. As shown
above, the key discursive nodal points that constitute the alliance of
indigenous peoples\' organizations and environmental civil society
organizations, and high profile environmental scholars and activists, is
[their shared articulation of themselves as respecting the sanctity of
Mother Earth, advocating for drastic emission cuts and nature-based
solutions to climate change]{.mark}]{.underline}. [[Geoengineering
is]{.mark} posited as [embodying the opposite of these values, as it
is]{.mark} seen as [expressive of colonial, extractivist mastery over
nature mentalities,]{.mark} [and as a legitimation device for the status
quo, providing the billionaires who fund it with the promise of a

who try to solve the problems they have created by over-exploiting
nature, through further, deliberate manipulation of it, where they stand
to benefit as the providers of the solutions to the problems they
created]{.mark}.]{.underline}

## AT: Climate Adv

### 1NC\-\-- Can't Solve

#### Geoengineering can't solve

**W**orld **W**ildlife **F**und **25**. Martin Siegert is a glaciologist
and polar scientist at the University of Exeter. ("Why Geoengineering
Isn't the Answer to Climate Change", World Wildlife Fund,
[[https://www.arcticwwf.org/the-circle/stories/why-geoengineering-isnt-the-answer-to-climate-change/]{.underline}](https://www.arcticwwf.org/the-circle/stories/why-geoengineering-isnt-the-answer-to-climate-change/))

What are some examples of the kinds of projects that are being proposed?

A well-known idea is to [[inject aerosols into]{.mark} the upper
[atmosphere]{.mark}]{.underline} to cause it to reflect sunlight rather
than allowing the sun's energy to hit the Earth's surface. It's very
controversial. It stems from the idea that if you have a volcanic
eruption, huge amounts of volcanic material can be pushed into the
atmosphere, and that can have a temporary but noticeable cooling effect.
So the idea is: What if we just did that ourselves, and did it to
permanently cool the planet? You might be able to do that. But the
[logistics behind it are [challenging]{.mark}]{.underline}, and it
[[doesn't resolve other aspects of global warming,]{.underline}]{.mark}
such as ocean acidification. In fact, it will contribute to further
ocean acidification. As an intervention, it would be colossally
irresponsible because we don't know what the consequences might be.

[[For]{.mark} the [Arctic]{.mark} in particular, [there are
projects]{.mark}]{.underline} that are [[trying to]{.mark} increase or
[enhance the albedo]{.mark}]{.underline}---or reflectivity---[of the sea
ice that exists, because that does the world a great favour by bouncing
solar energy back out into space rather than absorbing it.]{.underline}
Arctic sea ice is retreating really quickly, and the idea is that by
increasing the albedo, we can either preserve more sea ice or put
measures in place to grow sea ice artificially. There's no reason to
assume that you couldn't artificially grow sea ice at a really small
scale. There are some experiments that have tried to do that, and they
kind of worked. [[But the colossal scale of the Arctic Sea ice that you
would have to adjust makes the whole thing practically
impossible]{.underline}.]{.mark}

What risks do these [[geoengineering]{.mark} projects]{.underline} pose
for the Arctic?

Well, it depends on what you want to do. Many of them [[introduce risks
to the environment]{.mark}.]{.underline} Sometimes they're called
"unintended consequences," but that's a misnomer because we kind of know
what the consequences might be. Still, some people seem happy to go
along with them.

[[Suppose]{.underline}]{.mark} that [[thickening sea ice can
work]{.mark}]{.underline} and that we could bring thousands of devices
into the Arctic to do it. [[That would require a level of presence in
the Arctic]{.mark} that is [far in excess of what we have at the moment.
Consultation with Indigenous Peoples would need to
happen]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark} And who would make the decision to
do it? It is very difficult to see how we would ever have any governance
to support it on an international level.

[[In]{.mark} the [example of]{.mark} intentionally [putting a pollutant
into the Arctic system, the other risk is to the marine biology]{.mark}.
These are fragile ecosystems,]{.underline} and [[introducing anything
that might]{.underline} [interfere]{.underline}]{.mark} with them
[[could be felt permanently]{.mark},]{.underline} or at least for a very
long time.

Overall, [introducing people, industry and intentional pollutants into
the Arctic would make it a very different place from what it is right
now]{.underline}.

If these ideas pose such risks, [[why do you think they are being
considered]{.underline}]{.mark}?

When I started to see these ideas being presented at climate COPs and
other climate meetings without challenge, I realized that the people who
were accepting the ideas as feasible were doing so because that's how
they're presented. But I and others know that these are actually
unworkable.

There are also vested interests when it comes to geoengineering.
[[Suppose you're an oil]{.underline}]{.mark} and gas [[company]{.mark}
that is [making]{.mark}]{.underline} huge amounts of [[money as
people]{.underline}]{.mark} continue to [[burn fossil fuels. It would be
very tempting to support a geoengineering idea
that]{.underline}]{.mark}, frankly, [[will never
happen]{.underline}]{.mark}. There is just the pretence that they are
contributing to a solution by spending a little money on research, when
they know full well it will never actually happen. That is something I
think we all need to keep our eyes open for.

#### Geoengineering has a net negative effect

Samantha **Ibbot** **21**, senior communications officer for Agriculture
and Food at the European Environmental Bureau with a BSc in Biology at
the University of Nottingham. Abdul Malik is the leader of the Swiss
National Science Foundation and works at the Grantham Institute in
climatology with a PhD from the King Abdullah University of Science and
Technology. ("Solar geoengineering not a 'sensible rescue plan', say
scientists", Imperial,
[[https://www.imperial.ac.uk/news/213175/solar-geoengineering-sensible-rescue-plan-scientists]{.underline}](https://www.imperial.ac.uk/news/213175/solar-geoengineering-sensible-rescue-plan-scientists))

[New study indicates]{.underline} that [[reflecting solar energy back to
space]{.underline}]{.mark} in an attempt to reduce global warming [could
[cause more problems than it solves]{.mark}.]{.underline}

Increasing emissions of greenhouse gases into the atmosphere, resulting
in an accelerating rise in atmospheric temperature, are creating a
hugely [significant challenge to people and the planet]{.underline}.  

Scientists are looking into the plausibility of ideas that aim to limit
the effects of climate change, while carbon emissions from
humans burning fossil fuels continue to over-insulate the planet.
\'Solar geoengineering\' proposes to reflect incoming sunlight back into
space by deliberately releasing reflective particles into the upper
atmosphere where they block the sun\'s warming rays.

Providing deeper insight 

New research funded by the Swiss National Science Foundation, and led by
Dr Abdul Malik, formerly a researcher at the Grantham Institute --
Climate Change and the Environment, with colleagues at Imperial College
London and Zhejiang University in China, offers the most detailed
analysis yet and contributes to the ongoing Geoengineering Model
Intercomparison Project (GeoMIP), which investigates the likely effects
of geoengineering on the planet.

Dr Abdul Malik, lead author and former Postdoctoral Research Fellow at
the Grantham Institute, now a researcher at King Abdullah University of
Science and Technology.

The authors of the study, published in Atmospheric Chemistry and
Physics, [[simulated three versions of the Earth's
climate using high-powered computers]{.mark} running the UK Met Office's
climate model,]{.underline} called HadCM3L. These three experimental
Earths explored the consequences of increased atmospheric carbon dioxide
concentrations with and without solar geoengineering, to see what would
happen over a thousand years. These experiments are [[much longer than
previous]{.mark} such [studies]{.mark} and [provide deeper insight
into the potential implications of
solar geoengineering]{.mark}.]{.underline}

The [[first]{.mark} experiment [simulated]{.mark}]{.underline} the
climate before the time of the Industrial Revolution
(approximately 1750-1850), before humans were burning large amounts of
fossil fuels and increasing the amount of carbon dioxide in the
atmosphere, this is
known as [the '[pre-Industrial' period]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark} The
[[second envisaged]{.underline}]{.mark} an
[[atmosphere]{.underline}]{.mark} loaded [[with]{.mark} four times
more carbon dioxide than]{.underline} in the
[pre-Industrial]{.underline} period: This simulated
[a scenario where [no actions]{.mark} were [taken to cut today's carbon
emission]{.mark}s]{.underline}. The [[third]{.underline}]{.mark}
experiment [[simulated]{.mark} a world with four times the
pre-Industrial level of carbon dioxide as before, but with sunlight
reduced by 4% at the top of the Earth\'s atmosphere, which simulated
a [climate]{.mark} that had been [altered by solar
geoengineering.]{.mark} ]{.underline}

What does solar geoengineering mean for the climate? 

The [study found that [solar geoengineering could counterbalance the
warming in global mean]{.mark}]{.underline} (average) [[surface
temperature]{.underline}]{.mark} resulting from quadrupled carbon
dioxide. [[However]{.mark}, the [scientists also saw]{.mark}
a [significant increase in the number and intensity
of]{.mark}]{.underline} a naturally occurring climate phenomenon called
the [[El Niño]{.underline}]{.mark} Southern Oscillation (ENSO).

ENSO is an irregular cycle between two opposite phases known as 'El
Niño', a warming of the ocean surface in the equatorial Pacific, and 'La
Niña', a cooling of the ocean surface in the equatorial Pacific, both of
which result in changes to atmospheric circulation. ENSO is the subject
of intense focus for climate experts, owing to its ability to affect
global climate systems and consequences of this for fisheries,
agriculture, weather, and natural disasters worldwide.  

Dr Abdul Malik, now a postdoctoral researcher at King Abdullah
University of Science and Technology (KAUST) in Saudi Arabia,
said: \"[[More intense La Niña events and frequent occurrence of extreme
ENSO events can strongly impact temperature, precipitation]{.mark},
floods and [drought]{.mark} patterns [across the
globe]{.mark}]{.underline}\". 

Emeritus Professor Joanna Haigh, Distinguished Research Fellow at
Imperial and former Co-Director of the Grantham Institute, said: \"The
[[results]{.mark} of this study [indicate]{.mark}]{.underline} [that
solar [geoengineering can in no sense be viewed as a sensible rescue
plan]{.mark} due to the potential to severely impact on temperature,
precipitation, floods and drought patterns across the
globe]{.underline}.\"

The [study showed]{.underline} that [[El Niño]{.underline}]{.mark} event
t[hat occurred approximately every 3.3 years in]{.underline} the climate
of the [pre-Industrial]{.underline} period [[could occur more
frequently]{.mark} in]{.underline} the [solar geoengineered
climate - approximately every 2.9 years]{.underline}.
Similarly, [[extreme]{.underline}]{.mark} El Niño or La Niña
[[events]{.underline}]{.mark}, which might occur every 16-22 years in a
pre-Industrial climate [[could occur every 10-16
years]{.mark} in the geoengineered climate.]{.underline} Furthermore,
the most extreme La Niña events could become more intense in the
geoengineered climate than in the preindustrial climate, and that these
could more significantly impact the global climate system. 

\"This study is based on a single climate model with much longer climate
experiments than previous studies, which found no change in the ENSO
frequency in the geoengineered climate. However, more experiments, as
proposed by the GeoMIP project, need to be performed to understand
further the ENSO variability and its impacts in a geoengineered
climate,\" concluded co-author Professor Long Cao from Zhejiang
University, China. 

### 1NC\-\-- SRM/SAI can't solve

#### SRM cooling can't completely solve climate change

**NOAA 24**, National Oceanic and Atmospheric Association. ("Solar
radiation modification: NOAA State of the Science factsheet",
Climate.gov,
[[https://www.climate.gov/news-features/understanding-climate/solar-radiation-modification-noaa-state-science-factsheet]{.underline}](https://www.climate.gov/news-features/understanding-climate/solar-radiation-modification-noaa-state-science-factsheet))

Current State of SRM Research

Extensive research efforts are underway in the scientific community to
gain a comprehensive understanding of the feasibility, risks, benefits,
and negative consequences of possible SRM strategies to reduce surface
temperatures. SRM research centers on developing and studying a range of
potential future scenarios that combine emissions reductions, CDR, and
SRM to varying degrees and over varying timescales. In one such
scenario, commonly called "peak-shaving" (illustrated in Figure 2), SRM
would be implemented as a temporary method alongside strong GHG emission
cuts and aggressive CDR to stabilize global temperatures and offset some
of the most negative impacts of climate change.

Figure 2.  Schematic diagram representing the concept of peak-shaving.
Different lines illustrate the global mean temperatures for future
scenarios. Blue arrows represent the relative magnitude of the
temperature impact of stratospheric aerosol injection (SAI).

[[SRM deployments large enough to temporarily offset
climate]{.mark}]{.underline} change [[impacts could have substantial
risks]{.mark} and]{.underline} unintended and [unexpected consequences.
[Side effects]{.mark} of SRM are [driven by complex]{.mark} chemical,
radiative, and [dynamical interactions,]{.mark}]{.underline} such as
changes to the hydrologic cycle and clouds, or effects on ecosystems,
agricultural production, and the carbon cycle. Additional impacts on the
protective stratospheric ozone layer by SAI and alterations to the El
Niño Southern Oscillation by MCB are also possible. The potential risks
and benefits to human health and well-being and to ecosystems from SRM
need to be evaluated and assessed relative to those associated with
plausible trajectories of ongoing climate change without SRM (i.e.,
using risk vs. risk analysis). Current understanding of risks and
benefits is limited by uncertainties in the observations and modeling
tools used to examine SRM impacts. 

[[GHG]{.mark}-induced [warming and SRM]{.mark}-induced [cooling are
different physical mechanisms]{.mark}; therefore, [SRM would not]{.mark}
simply [undo GHG]{.mark}-induced [warming and cannot reverse all climate
changes everywhere]{.mark}.]{.underline} In fact, [[effects of climate
change on regional weather]{.underline}]{.mark} patterns [w[ill not be
fully mitigated]{.mark} by SRM]{.underline}. Some regions could
experience cooler or wetter conditions, and others warmer or drier
conditions, compared to a climate without SRM. [In addition[, ocean
acidification will persist]{.mark} unabated [if GHG emissions
continue]{.mark}. ]{.underline}

Importantly, both the [[effectiveness of SRM]{.underline}]{.mark} in
offsetting the impacts of global warming and the potential unintended
consequences [[depend on how and when SRM is
deployed]{.underline}]{.mark}. Recent studies on SRM indicate that some
of the negative impacts can be reduced through design of an optimal
injection strategy (i.e. location, magnitude, and timing of injection). 

Substantial modeling efforts to understand the processes and impacts of
both SAI and MCB are ongoing. These efforts have demonstrated the basic
efficacy of SRM for cooling Earth's surface temperatures, while also
identifying a number of gaps and uncertainties in our current
understanding, as well as potential unintended outcomes.

[[Many]{.mark} of the [processes most important for
understanding]{.mark}]{.underline} SRM approaches---such as those that
control the formation of clouds and aerosols---[[are]{.mark} among the
[most uncertain components of the climate system. Climate models differ
in simulating]{.mark}]{.underline} large-scale aerosol climate
[effects,]{.underline} including on surface temperatures, [[due to
variations in how]{.mark} aerosol [processes]{.mark}, atmospheric
transport and mixing, and physics [are represented]{.mark}.]{.underline}
There remain large uncertainties as to the changes in large scale
circulation, regional climate, air quality, and weather, associated with
SRM. Research on low-level clouds has also revealed complex interactions
with aerosols, so the [[net climate effect of injecting aerosols into
the lower atmosphere for MCB is difficult to
project.]{.mark}]{.underline} Improving understanding of these
fundamental processes requires expanded atmospheric observations and
innovations in modeling.

#### Geoengineering is a false solution

Mary **Church** and Rossella **Recupero**, 20**24** -- Geoengineering
Campaign Manager and Communications Campaign Specialist at the Center
for International Environmental Law. "Why Geoengineering is a False
Solution to the Climate Crisis," Center for International Environmental
Law,
<https://www.ciel.org/why-geoengineering-is-a-false-solution-to-the-climate-crisis>
// Astra4

Behind the shiny facade of these **[[highly
speculative]{.underline}]{.mark}** "techno-fixes," geoengineering is
fraught with risks, uncertainties, [and]{.mark} dangers that [[threaten
to **delay real climate action** and cause **more harm than
good**]{.underline}]{.mark}.

What Is Geoengineering?

Geoengineering refers to large or planetary-scale interventions in the
Earth's atmosphere, oceans, and land to counteract some of the effects
of climate change. These methods range from reflecting sunlight to
removing carbon dioxide from the atmosphere.

Crucially, **[[none tackle]{.mark} the [root causes of]{.mark} the
[climate crisis]{.mark}]{.underline}**, the primary driver of which is
the production and burning of fossil fuels.

Geoengineering Categories

Solar Radiation Modification Or Manipulation (SRM)

SRM techniques attempt to reflect sunlight back into space to cool
Earth. Methods include releasing large amounts of tiny reflective
particles (e.g., sulfur dioxide) into the stratosphere, deploying
mirrors in space, or modifying clouds, ice, and plants to make them more
reflective.

Carbon Dioxide Removal (CDR)

CDR techniques theorize removing carbon from the atmosphere on a massive
scale. Methods range from sucking carbon straight out of the air to the
large-scale burning of trees for energy, capturing and storing the
emissions, and even dumping minerals in the ocean or developing
monoculture seaweed mega-farms to increase carbon uptake.

Other approaches

Other theories include slowing ice melt by pumping seawater onto polar
and glacial ice to thicken it, spreading billions of tiny reflective
beads on the surface of the Arctic or glacial ice to increase its
reflectivity, and building a giant curtain in the ocean to block warmer
waters.

Why is Geoengineering a Bad Idea?

It won't fix the climate crisis

Geoengineering [technologies]{.mark} [do nothing to address the drivers
of the climate crisis, and [all]{.mark} [come with major risks and
unknowns]{.mark}]{.underline}. For example, [solar]{.mark}
geoengineering is [**[inherently unpredictable]{.mark}** and [risks
further destabilizing]{.mark} an already destabilized climate
system]{.underline}. [[Models]{.mark} [show]{.mark} that [it would have
an uneven effect regionally]{.mark} [and]{.mark} could **[exacerbate
climate change]{.mark}** in countries on the front line of the
crisis.]{.underline} What's more, it [[carries]{.mark} the [risk of
'termination shock']{.mark} where **[temperatures suddenly
spiral]{.mark}**]{.underline} if, for whatever reason, deployment is
paused or stopped. Meanwhile, [[no marine]{.mark} carbon dioxide
[removal techniques]{.mark} have been [proven effective in]{.mark} the
[long-term removal and storage of CO2]{.mark}, [and]{.mark} some
[could]{.mark} [undermine]{.mark} the [ocean's ability to sequester
carbon]{.mark}]{.underline}.

It's a **[[dangerous distraction]{.underline}]{.mark}** that undermines
real climate solutions

The illusion that geoengineering can help "fix" climate change is a
dangerous distraction from the real solutions to the crisis and
**[[risks prolonging fossil fuel dependence]{.underline}]{.mark}**. In
fact, geoengineering technologies are inherently unpredictable and pose
new, significant, unprecedented risks to the fragile ecosystems that
sustain life on Earth, which are our best allies in the fight against
the crisis. What's more, [[relying]{.mark} [on]{.mark} the [promise of
future speculative]{.mark} [technologies instead of implementing real
solutions today]{.mark} will **[lead to]{.mark} an [overshoot of]{.mark}
the [critical climate threshold of 1.5ºC]{.mark} and [lock in
catastrophic climate change]{.mark}**]{.underline}. By undermining
transformative, justice-centered solutions to the climate crisis,
geoengineering approaches also risk entrenching inequitable political
and social power and perpetuating neocolonialism.

### 2NC\-\-- SAI can't solve

#### Geoengineering is ineffective in the Arctic and has extreme side effects

Martin **Siegert et al. 24**, glaciologist at the University of Exeter,
PhD from Cambridge University, famous for developing technology that
surveyed half of Antarctica. Heïdi Sevestre is a glaciologist with a PhD
from Svalbard University, a recipient of the Shackleton Medal for her

[[https://www.researchgate.net/publication/385550908_Safeguarding_the_polar_regions_from_dangerous_geoengineering]{.underline}](https://www.researchgate.net/publication/385550908_Safeguarding_the_polar_regions_from_dangerous_geoengineering))

In the absence of any field assessments and pilot studies, and [based on
a variety of modelling investigations, the]{.underline}
Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change ([IPCC]{.underline}, 2023)
[has noted large uncertainties in the effectiveness of deployment of
solar geoengineering]{.underline} (De Coninck et al., 2018), substantial
[knowledge gaps regarding its cost]{.underline}-effectiveness, [and a

[It has been suggested that aerosols could be injected into the
stratosphere using balloons, airships, and/or artillery. However, the
most efficient method is]{.underline} likely to involve [a fleet
of]{.underline} custom-built and/or [specially modified
aircraft]{.underline} (Moriyama et al., 2017). Smith and Wagner (2018)

globally]{.underline} (OAG 2024)---[could be required for a lasting
effect]{.underline}. Smith et al. (2024) recently highlighted the Boeing
777F as a potential candidate, albeit requiring substantial
modifications, with a fleet of 90 planes focusing on deployment in the
polar regions. Deployment strategies include aerosol injection: (i) in
the tropics or subtropics (at or equatorward of 30°), (ii) uniformly
across all latitudes, and (iii) in the sub-polar or polar regions (at or
poleward of 60°). Typically, the tropopause is lowest in altitude in the
polar regions, facilitating larger payloads and requiring fewer planes
to achieve a given injection mass (Lee et al., 2021; Moriyama et al.,
2017; Smith, 2020).

[[SAIs would need a highly specific deployment to have significant
impact in polar regions]{.underline}]{.mark} (Goddard et al., 2023; Lee
et al., 2023). The [[radiative forcing]{.underline}]{.mark} from
stratospheric aerosols [[depends on local]{.mark} incoming [solar
radiation and top-of-atmosphere albedo. Polar regions are less
responsive to aerosol injection during sunlit
periods]{.mark}]{.underline} because of their lower insolation and
higher albedo produced by ice and snow (Govindasamy & Caldeira, 2000;
Tilmes et al.,228 2018). Furthermore, [[injections]{.mark} are totally
i[neffective during the winter months in the polar regions. The
Brewer-Dobson circulation]{.mark}]{.underline}, characterized by rising
air in the tropics and descending air in the mid and high latitudes,
[[affects the distribution and lifetime]{.mark} of stratospheric
aerosols.]{.underline} [[Aerosols injected at high

[[If deployed at the necessary scale, SAI would only have discernible
effects]{.mark}]{.underline} (within climate variability) [[over 20

time]{.mark}]{.underline} horizon over [[which]{.underline}]{.mark} the
[[effects of sharp mitigation by reduced GHG emissions would be
seen]{.underline}]{.mark} (Tilmes et al., 2020).

['[Termination shock']{.mark}]{.underline} is the rapid and severe
warming that could occur by the unmasking of ongoing GHG emissions if
any future large-scale deployment of solar geoengineering were halted
(Parker and Irvine, 2018). The [sudden cessation would
remove]{.underline} the [reflective aerosols]{.underline} from the

would245 occur, including a rapid increase in global mean precipitation

The [readiness of SAI for implementation varies greatly between
polar-specific and global deployments]{.underline} (Diamond et al.,
2023). [[If]{.underline}]{.mark} a launch [[decision were made by 2030,

2040]{.underline}]{.mark}. On a global scale, if a decision to proceed

Negative consequences

[In]{.underline} the [polar regions, atmospheric injections would be
ineffective in winter owing to the lack of sunlight, and instead could
lead to residual warming in winter]{.underline} ([due to the blanketing
effect of cloud cover]{.underline}), potentially [altering]{.underline}
the high-latitude [seasonal cycle]{.underline} (Jiang et al., 2019;
Banerjee et al., 2021), with potential consequences for the water cycle
through atmospheric drying. It could also cause stratospheric heating,
which may alter atmospheric circulation patterns, leading to wintertime
warming over northern Eurasia and potentially other unintended
consequences (Banerjee et al., 2021; Jones et al., 2021).

[[SAIs would not address non-temperature related effects of GHG
emissions, such as ocean acidification]{.mark}.]{.underline} Rather
[[they could exacerbate acidification, especially if
sulfates]{.underline}]{.mark} (the original "acid rain" emissions source
from coal-fired power) [[were used]{.underline}]{.mark}, with consequent
negative impacts on ecosystems and economies. Both the Arctic and
Southern Oceans already show seasonally corrosive conditions for shelled
life due to today's atmospheric CO2 levels, and shell damage from
increasing acidification was observed from samples taken as early as the
2000s (Bednarsek et al, 2020). Year-round corrosive conditions are
projected to spread throughout much of the polar marine ecosystem with
even moderate emissions pathways, at CO2 levels above 450 ppm (IPCC,
2019). Ocean acidification is also a very long-term problem, with a
return to today's pH levels taking 30,000--50,000 years (Honisch et al,
2012). Indeed, acidification rates are higher today than at any point in
the last 300 million years (Pelejero et al, 2010). This raises the
specter of extinctions for many polar marine shelled animals and the
economically important species that depend on them, such as krill and
cod (AMAP, 2018; Green et al, 2021).

Injections using sulfate aerosols can also lead to chemical reactions in
the stratosphere that deplete ozone (Tilmes et al., 2008). Ozone
depletion increases the amount of ultraviolet radiation reaching the
Earth's surface, causing harmful effects on human health (see below),
ecosystems, and biodiversity as a whole (Moch et al., 2023; Zarnetske et
al., 2021).

[[Using SAI to]{.mark} differentially [cool one polar region]{.mark}
alone [could disrupt global climate patterns.]{.mark} An [imbalance in
cooling between]{.mark} the [hemispheres could shift the Intertropical
Convergence Zone]{.mark},]{.underline} potentially interfering with
seasons (Haywood et al., 2013; Kravitz et al., 2017; Määttänen et al.,
2024) and reducing rainfall in some regions -- [thereby [affecting water
availability and agriculture]{.mark}]{.underline} (Tilmes et al., 2022).

The [[reduction in sunlight]{.mark} reaching the Earth's surface could
reduce photosynthesis and agricultural productivity, as occurred in 1815
when]{.underline} the eruption of Indonesia's [Mount Tambora eruption
released sulfate aerosols into the atmosphere]{.underline} (Oppenheimer,
2003). Reduced sunlight could negatively affect crop yields, threatening
food security and potentially leading to higher food prices. This
[[would disproportionately affect poorer regions that rely heavily on
agriculture]{.mark}]{.underline} (Wang et al., 2023). Uneven effects of
SAI might potentially harm those already most vulnerable to disturbances
in food production, and with less means to protect themselves if
benefits to more powerful populations are optimized (Honegger et al.,
2021). The effects of SAI on ocean circulation are also uncertain.
Crucially, some models suggest the Atlantic Meridional Overturning
Circulation (AMOC) could be weakened, with implications for regional
climates and weather patterns (Tilmes et al., 2020).

[Concerns]{.underline} also [exist regarding the effect of aerosol
inhalation on human health]{.underline} (Carslon et al., 2022). Sulfuric
acid aerosols can irritate the respiratory system, causing coughing,
airway irritation and inflammation and, in severe cases, respiratory
distress. [Chronic exposure has been associated with an increased risk
of laryngeal cancer]{.underline} owing to long-term irritation and
inflammation. This could lead to increased healthcare costs and add a
burden on medical infrastructure, with particular risks for vulnerable
populations (Effiong and Neitzel, 2016a,b).

#### Absent a halting of emissions, geoengineering can't completely mitigate warming

Tapio **Schneider et al. 20,** climate scientist and professor of
environmental science and engineering at the California Institute of
Technology. Colleen M. Kaul is a researcher with a PhD in aerospace
engineering from UT Austin. Kyle Pressel is a computational climate
scientist at Pacific Northwest National Laboratory. ("Solar
geoengineering may not prevent strong warming from direct effects of CO2
on stratocumulus cloud cover," Proceedings of the National Academy of
Sciences of the United States of America,
[[https://www.pnas.org/doi/full/10.1073/pnas.2003730117]{.underline}](https://www.pnas.org/doi/full/10.1073/pnas.2003730117))

- Even if the exact amount of carbon dioxide that would cause cloud
  breakup is unclear, it is uncontested that it will happen.

Discussion

Our results show that [[solar geoengineering may not be a
fail-safe]{.underline}]{.mark} option [[to counter]{.mark} global
[warming]{.mark}]{.underline}. At least [in]{.underline} extreme and
[idealized]{.underline} solar geoengineering [scenarios, rising GHG
concentrations can eventually lead to breakup of subtropical
stratocumulus decks]{.underline}. Because our model assumes that the
extra solar energy absorbed when subtropical stratocumulus decks break
up is spread uniformly across the globe, the tropical SST increase of
around 5 K can be taken as a proxy for the global warming triggered by
stratocumulus breakup. That is, [[even with solar geoengineering,
strong]{.mark} global [warming can occur if CO~2~ continues to
accumulate in the atmosphere]{.mark} to reach concentrations more than
four times today's. It would take over a century to reach such CO~2~
concentrations,]{.underline} even if emissions increase further
([[42]{.underline}](https://www.pnas.org/doi/full/10.1073/pnas.2003730117#core-collateral-r42)).
Extending solar geoengineering to such high CO~2~ concentrations is not
currently considered. And it is possible that the stratocumulus breakup
and attendant warming can be delayed to yet higher CO~2~ concentrations
if solar radiation is managed even more aggressively, so that there is
no surface temperature increase initially. [[If stratocumulus breakup
eventually occurs, the warming it triggers dramatically amplifies the
termination shock that would occur if solar geoengineering were stopped
thereafter]{.mark}.]{.underline}

Several caveats are in order. The [[thinning of stratocumulus clouds
under increasing CO~2~ concentrations is robust and well
established]{.mark}]{.underline}
([[24]{.underline}](https://www.pnas.org/doi/full/10.1073/pnas.2003730117#core-collateral-r24)).
The amplifying feedback between thinning clouds and a warming sea
surface is likewise rooted in well-understood physics, and that it can
run away and lead to stratocumulus breakup is physically plausible. The
mechanisms involved are known to play a role in the
stratocumulus--cumulus transition in the present climate
([[21]{.underline}](https://www.pnas.org/doi/full/10.1073/pnas.2003730117#core-collateral-r21)).
However, [[it remains unclear at which]{.mark} CO~2~ [concentrations
breakup]{.mark} of stratocumulus [may occur.]{.mark}]{.underline} Our
simulations with different subsidence rates illustrate some of the
difficulties of quantifying the critical CO~2~ concentrations for
stratocumulus breakup. But because our modeling setup lacks explicit
representation of spatial heterogeneity and of temporal variations, such
as the seasonal cycle and large-scale weather variability
(meteorological noise), the real uncertainties are even greater. Spatial
heterogeneity means that stratocumulus breakup, if it occurs in reality,
would occur first in regions and seasons in which the stratocumulus
decks are close to the stability threshold, likely at the margins of
current stratocumulus regions
([[27]{.underline}](https://www.pnas.org/doi/full/10.1073/pnas.2003730117#core-collateral-r27)).
The lack of spatial heterogeneity and temporal variability in our
simulations make it difficult to quantify more precisely the range of
CO~2~ concentrations over which stratocumulus breakup may occur, or when
the clouds reform after CO~2~ concentrations are lowered. The sources of
noise neglected in our simulation may also reduce the width of the
hysteresis loop and render the transitions between the states with and
without stratocumulus decks less sharp as a function of CO~2~
concentration than they are in our simulations
([[27]{.underline}](https://www.pnas.org/doi/full/10.1073/pnas.2003730117#core-collateral-r27)).
These are some of the limitations that come with the idealizations that
were necessary to clearly illustrate the mechanisms involved and make
this study computationally feasible; we plan to overcome these
limitations in future work. Furthermore, we have not addressed the
question of which, if any, solar geoengineering method may provide
sufficient reductions in absorbed solar radiation to offset the
radiative forcing of quadrupled (or higher) CO~2~ concentrations; it is
unclear whether such extensive solar geoengineering would be feasible
([[12]{.underline}](https://www.pnas.org/doi/full/10.1073/pnas.2003730117#core-collateral-r12),
[[14]{.underline}](https://www.pnas.org/doi/full/10.1073/pnas.2003730117#core-collateral-r14)).

[[Caveats and limitations notwithstanding, the results illustrate a
hitherto-unrecognized risk of solar geoengineering]{.mark}. It [stems
from]{.mark} the [effects of elevated CO~2~ concentrations on cloud
cover]{.mark}, [which]{.mark} generally reduce or [thin cloud cover and
are amplified by]{.mark}]{.underline} the [[surface warming that
results]{.mark} from reduced]{.underline} or thinned [cloud
cover]{.underline}
([[16]{.underline}](https://www.pnas.org/doi/full/10.1073/pnas.2003730117#core-collateral-r16)--[[20]{.underline}](https://www.pnas.org/doi/full/10.1073/pnas.2003730117#core-collateral-r20)).
[[These effects are **difficult to offset by manipulating** the amount
of **solar radiation** Earth absorbs.]{.mark} The idealizations of our
study bring limitations with them, especially with regard to
quantitative statements about the CO~2~ concentrations]{.underline} at
which stratocumulus breakup occurs. [Nonetheless]{.underline}, [the
results highlight]{.underline} that the relative [risks and benefits of
solar geoengineering remain insufficiently understood and quantified. It
is imperative to understand]{.underline} and quantify
[these]{.underline} risks and benefits [better]{.underline}. Doing so
will require developing climate models that capture clouds, and
especially low clouds, more accurately than do current models
([[43]{.underline}](https://www.pnas.org/doi/full/10.1073/pnas.2003730117#core-collateral-r43)).

### Ice pumps can't solve

#### Sea ice thickening on an impactful scale is logistically impossible

Martin **Siegert et al. 24**, glaciologist at the University of Exeter,
PhD from Cambridge University, famous for developing technology that
surveyed half of Antarctica. Heïdi Sevestre is a glaciologist with a PhD
from Svalbard University, a recipient of the Shackleton Medal for her
field research, and a science communicator. Michael Bentley is a polar
geoscientist and a professor at Durham University who focuses on
Antarctica and Greenland. Julie Brigham-Grette is a glacial geologist
and professor at the University of Massachusetts Amherst who focuses on
the Arctic. ("Safeguarding the polar regions from dangerous
geoengineering," ResearchGate,
[[https://www.researchgate.net/publication/385550908_Safeguarding_the_polar_regions_from_dangerous_geoengineering]{.underline}](https://www.researchgate.net/publication/385550908_Safeguarding_the_polar_regions_from_dangerous_geoengineering))

Effectiveness and feasibility

The [[main issue]{.mark}]{.underline} for this idea [[is]{.mark} not
whether the technique itself can work]{.underline} (although there are
many reasons to be skeptical about this) [but [whether it is feasible
for use at the scale necessary and in the time available.
Long-term]{.mark}]{.underline} average [[Arctic]{.underline}]{.mark} sea
[[ice]{.mark} extent v[aries between summer and winter from 5--16
million km2. If]{.mark}]{.underline} we assume [[a single device could
cover 1 km2]{.underline}]{.mark} (that would be an enormous, arguably
implausible, machine) \>[[10 million devices would be needed]{.mark} to
cover today's winter sea ice. [If]{.mark}]{.underline} we accept Desch
et al.'s claim that [[only 10% cover is needed, this would necessitate 1
million devices. If]{.underline}]{.mark} we then assume [[1,000 devices
could be deployed every year]{.underline}]{.mark} (another colossal
undertaking) [[it would still take 1000 years]{.mark} to cover 10% of
the present Arctic sea ice cover]{.underline}. In other words, [[sea ice
thickening is]{.mark} simply [not feasible for use at a scale and rate
that would be meaningful for]{.mark} sea-ice
[protection]{.mark}.]{.underline} Notwithstanding this critical failure,
[the [scale of implementing]{.mark} the method [would require an
unprecedented level of human presence in the High Arctic for
construction]{.mark}]{.underline} in the first instance
[[and]{.underline}]{.mark} for [[maintenance]{.underline}]{.mark}
thereafter. [[Other]{.underline}]{.mark} inherent
[[challenges]{.underline}]{.mark} may [render the idea
infeasible]{.underline} even at a local scale, [[such as]{.mark} the
difficulty in [keeping the pumps in place]{.mark}]{.underline}
(considering the strong sea ice drift), the [[need for year-round
maintenance]{.mark} over such a wide area]{.underline}, the [[sinking of
pumps if sea ice melts]{.mark} or breaks up,]{.underline} and governance
issues---especially considering the Arctic Ocean is becoming an
increasingly congested space.

### Solar Geoengineering Fails

#### Termination worse than status quo

Christopher H. **Trisos** et al., 20**18** -- Research Chair at African
Climate and Development Initiative, Doctorate in Zoology, MSc. In
Integrative Bioscience, BSc. In Ecology and Economics. "Potentially
dangerous consequences for biodiversity of solar geoengineering
implementation and termination," ResearchGate,
[[https://www.researchgate.net/publication/322643124_Potentially_dangerous_consequences_for_biodiversity_of_solar_geoengineering_implementation_and_termination]{.underline}](https://www.researchgate.net/publication/322643124_Potentially_dangerous_consequences_for_biodiversity_of_solar_geoengineering_implementation_and_termination),
DOI:10.1038/s41559-017-0431-0 // Astra4

Sudden termination of geoengineering causes [[extremely rapid
temperature velocities]{.mark}
(Fig. [2b](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#Fig2)) with
the predicted median velocity [on land]{.mark} (10.4 km yr^−1^) **[more
than four times greater]{.mark}**, [and]{.mark} the median
[ocean]{.mark} velocity (12.8 km yr^--1^) **[more than six times
greater]{.mark}** than recent climate change
(]{.underline}Fig. [[2c]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#Fig2) and
Supplementary
Table [[1]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#MOESM1)).
[[Temperature velocities at termination]{.mark} also [far exceed those
predicted for future climate change without
geoengineering]{.mark}]{.underline}
(Fig. [[2d]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#Fig2)).
Compared to RCP4.5, the predicted median increase in the migration speed
required for species to track moving temperatures is 8.0 km yr^−1^ in
the ocean and 6.4 km yr^−1^ on land, which represent 2.7- and 2.8-fold
increases, respectively. The most rapid velocities occur where
temperature changes are high and spatial gradients in temperature are
low. Temperature velocities at termination [are **[most extreme in
tropical oceans]{.mark}, the [biodiversity-rich]{.mark}** Amazon Basin,
Africa, Eurasia [and polar regions]{.mark}.]{.underline}

At the global scale, the differences between geoengineering and RCP4.5
are less for precipitation than for temperature
(Fig. [[1]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#Fig1)).
This is partly because, compared to temperature, precipitation
velocities show much stronger differences among regions in their
response (positive or negative) to each phase of geoengineering
(Fig. [[2e,f]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#Fig2) and
Supplementary
Fig. [[1]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#MOESM1)).
Given these strong regional differences, the predicted median velocity
of precipitation on land for implementation is --0.5 km yr^--1^,
indicative of the relatively weak global drying trend. [Precipitation
velocities for termination show]{.mark} a [reversed pattern]{.mark} to
implementation, and are only weakly correlated with temperature
velocities for termination (*r* = 0.09). Sudden implementation reduces
precipitation with high negative velocities in the Amazon, northern
Europe and Asia, Australia and southeast Africa
(Fig. [[2h]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#Fig2)).
This El Niño-like drying and warming would be likely to
[[increase]{.mark} the [incidence and intensity of]{.mark} pan-tropical
[forest fires]{.mark} (as in true El Niños), [increasing CO~2~ emissions
and decreasing air quality]{.mark}, with [negative impacts on public
health and
biodiversity]{.mark}[31](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#ref-CR31)]{.underline}^, ^[[32]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#ref-CR32).

Temporal trends in temperature and precipitation over ten-year periods,
and [the [resulting]{.mark} [velocities]{.mark} of climate change, are
[more variable than for longer time periods]{.mark}]{.underline}. This
is because ten-year trends are more strongly influenced by sub-decadal
temperature fluctuations and the phase of phenomena such as the El
Niño/Southern Oscillation. Therefore, we tested whether [climate
velocities]{.mark} for the ten-year periods [[following
implementation]{.mark} (2020--2029) and termination (2070--2079) [of
geoengineering]{.mark} are [predicted to exceed climate
velocities]{.mark} for all ten-year intervals [in]{.mark} both the
[historical record]{.mark}]{.underline} and in RCP4.5 climate forecasts.
The median for global ocean temperature velocity for both implementation
and termination is predicted to exceed the most extreme temperature
velocities for all ten-year intervals in the recent historical record
(Fig. [[3a]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#Fig3)).
Furthermore, the probability of predicted ten-year ocean temperature
velocities for RCP4.5 being below the median for termination is high
(0.95). Similarly, for temperature velocities on land, median velocity
projections for both implementation and termination are more extreme
than in the historical record
(Fig. [[3b]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#Fig3)).
The probability of predicted RCP4.5 velocities being less than the
median for termination is 0.90. In all cases for temperature, the
extremes of global temperature velocity projections for implementation
and termination are at least 10 km yr^--1^ greater than RCP4.5 on the
same ten-year timescales. In contrast, differences between global
estimates of precipitation velocity for geoengineering and for ten-year
windows for either historical data or RCP4.5 forecasts are much less
pronounced
(Fig. [[3c]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#Fig3)),
which is indicative of greater variability in the precipitation response
to geoengineering
(Figs. [[1]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#Fig1) and [[2]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#Fig2)).

#### Termination leads to species extinction -- empirics prove

Christopher H. **Trisos** et al., 20**18** -- Research Chair at African
Climate and Development Initiative, Doctorate in Zoology, MSc. In

[[https://www.researchgate.net/publication/322643124_Potentially_dangerous_consequences_for_biodiversity_of_solar_geoengineering_implementation_and_termination]{.underline}](https://www.researchgate.net/publication/322643124_Potentially_dangerous_consequences_for_biodiversity_of_solar_geoengineering_implementation_and_termination),
DOI:10.1038/s41559-017-0431-0 // Astra4

[[Across terrestrial and marine biodiversity hotspots]{.mark}, sudden
**[termination]{.mark} [results in extreme temperature
velocities]{.mark} that are 2--[4 times faster than]{.mark} those for
either [historical data or future climate change without
geoengineering]{.mark}**, and are [highest in tropical marine

Supplementary
Table [[1]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#MOESM1)).
High temperature velocities in tropical regions may result from smaller

(Fig. [[2]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#Fig2) and
Supplementary
Figs. [[2]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#MOESM1),[[6]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#MOESM1)).
Thus, the effect of high temperature velocities on biodiversity could be
partly mitigated if many species are far from their upper thermal
limits. However[, [tropical species]{.mark} generally [have narrower
thermal tolerances than]{.mark} their [temperate
counterparts]{.mark}]{.underline} and are often near or above optimal
temperatures, making [climate]{.mark} tracking more [important
for]{.mark} their
[survival[[37]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#ref-CR37)]{.mark}^--^[[39]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#ref-CR39).
[[Extreme climate velocities from geoengineering]{.mark} termination
thus [represent]{.mark} a potentially [**acute threat to species
survival** in]{.mark} the [most biodiverse regions]{.mark} on
Earth]{.underline}.

Mean estimates of [species-range movements]{.mark} [in response to
recent warming]{.mark} for arthropods, birds, fish, reptiles and mammals
(1.7 km yr^−1^)[[18]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#ref-CR18) are
4--[[**7 times slower** than predicted]{.mark} temperature [velocities
from]{.mark} sudden [termination]{.mark}]{.underline}. Furthermore, in a

termination]{.mark} in mammal biodiversity hotspots (9.8 km yr^−1^) is
faster than the [median dispersal rate for **93% of species** and faster

[**majority of
species**[19](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#ref-CR19)]{.mark}]{.underline} (Supplementary
Fig. [[7]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#MOESM1)).
Given that mammals have relatively high dispersal ability, this result
suggests [that [less dispersive taxa]{.mark} may be **[even more at risk
of extinction]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Indeed, the association of regions
of low endemism with high climate velocity since the Last Glacial
Maximum has been shown to be strongest for poorly dispersing amphibian
taxa, indicative of increased extinctions when poorly dispersing species
fail to track climate
change[[23]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#ref-CR23).
Thus, while differences in climate velocity between terrestrial hotspots
for some taxa are small, that amphibian biodiversity hotspots have the
highest temperature velocities from sudden termination suggests that
increased extinction risks would be especially severe for this group
(Fig. [[5c]{.underline}](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41559-017-0431-0#Fig5)).
Further work is needed to link climate velocities with species-specific
dispersal rates and future dispersal cost pathways to obtain more
detailed estimates of possible [extinctions]{.mark}, in order to improve
forecasts of the [[consequences of geoengineering]{.underline}]{.mark}
for biodiversity.

#### Termination cooks (literally)

Modelling a global shift

To understand how the rapid termination of solar generation could affect
wildlife, the [[researchers used models to compare changes in
temperature and rainfall]{.mark} [under]{.mark} a [scenario]{.mark} in
which [SRM runs from 2020 until 2070 to]{.mark} a [scenario]{.mark}
[with no geoengineering]{.mark} [and]{.mark} an [intermediate level
of]{.mark} greenhouse gas [emissions]{.mark}]{.underline} (RCP4.5).

The researchers then calculated the "climate velocities" of each
scenario, which quantifies the speed and direction of shifting climates.

The results are shown on the charts below, which show the change in
temperature velocity for (a) a modelled scenario showing the
introduction of SRM, (b) the termination of SRM, (c) the current climate
between 1960 and 2014 and (d) an unengineered world with moderate
emissions.

In the chart, deep red signifies a rapid increase in the rate of
temperature rise, while blue shows rapidly decreasing temperature.

![A group of images of different colored maps AI-generated content may
be incorrect.](media/image2.png){width="6.5in"
height="3.120833333333333in"}

The change in temperature velocity under (a) a modelled scenario
including the introduction of solar geoengineering, (b) the termination
of solar geoengineering, (c ) the current climate between 1960 and 2014
and (d) a world without solar geoengineering with moderate emissions
(RCP4.5). Red shading shows increasing temperatures, while blue shows
decreases; the darker the shading, the faster the rate of change.
Source: Trisos et al. (2018)

The [results show]{.mark} that [while]{.mark} the [implementation
of]{.mark} solar [geoengineering]{.mark} [could cause]{.mark}
[temperatures to fall]{.mark} [fairly quickly]{.mark}, [[sudden
termination]{.mark} could [cause rapid increases as temperatures
rebound]{.mark}]{.underline}.

The [[rate of change in temperature under]{.mark} the
[termination]{.mark} of SRM could be two to **[four times larger than
those caused by climate change itself]{.mark}**]{.underline}, the
researchers conclude. The study [also]{.mark} finds [[similar]{.mark} --
though not as drastic -- [changes on rainfall]{.mark}.]{.underline}

This [large change]{.mark} in environmental conditions could **[[leave
many species unable to adapt and]{.mark} at a [high risk of
extinction]{.mark}]{.underline}**, the researchers say.

[Animals that do not find it easy to adapt to new environments, such as
[species found]{.mark} in [tropical rainforests and small island
habitats]{.mark}, will be the [least able to adapt]{.mark} to these
changes, they add. The [risk]{.mark} could be [highest for]{.mark}
slow-moving [amphibians]{.mark},]{.underline} the researchers note in
their paper:

"While differences in climate velocity between terrestrial hotspots for
some taxa \[animal groups\] are small, that amphibian biodiversity
hotspots have the highest temperature velocities from sudden termination
suggests that increased extinction risks would be especially severe for
this group."

In other words, a large number of amphibians are found in areas that are
expected to be most affected by a sudden SRM termination.

The new research makes an important contribution to the "broader
conversation" surrounding the risks of solar geoengineering, says Dr Ben
Kravitz, a climate scientist from the Pacific Northwest National
Laboratory, who was not involved in the new research. He tells Carbon
Brief:

"Although there are many issues that arise when one talks about
ecosystems, to the best of my knowledge, this study the first
quantitative look at what might happen to biodiversity under different
scenarios of geoengineering. And, importantly, the **[[study]{.mark} was
[done using multiple climate models]{.mark}]{.underline}**, meaning [we
have estimates of]{.mark} [how]{.mark} [robust]{.mark} the [model
responses]{.mark} [are]{.mark}."

#### Many ways it can fail

Christopher H. **Trisos** et al., 20**18** -- Research Chair at African
Climate and Development Initiative, Doctorate in Zoology, MSc. In
Integrative Bioscience, BSc. In Ecology and Economics. "Potentially
dangerous consequences for biodiversity of solar geoengineering
implementation and termination," ResearchGate ,
DOI:10.1038/s41559-017-0431-0 // Astra4

Geoengineering could be implemented and terminated either gradually or
rapidly. Here we have chosen to investigate a scenario with both rapid
implementation and termination to explore the upper bound of the
exposure of biodiversity to climate change impacts at either end of a
geoengineering deployment. Rapid implementation is plausible, as one or
a small number of nations could take action given the current lack of
regulatory constraints12,13. [**[Rapid termination]{.mark} is also
[feasible given]{.mark} the [weakness]{.mark}** [of]{.mark} the [global
climate regulatory regime8]{.mark}]{.underline}, 12,13. [[Interstate
conflict]{.mark} could cause geoengineering to fail, [and]{.mark}
[unintended negative consequences or regionally severe climate
events]{.mark} such as extreme droughts could [force rapid
termination]{.mark}]{.underline}, even if direct attribution of these
events to geoengineering could not be demonstrated3, 8, 13,14.
[Scholars]{.mark} of climate politics and policy have [noted]{.mark}
that the [**[challenge of sustaining]{.mark}** the
climate-change-offsetting effects of geoengineering and the risks of
termination [are]{.mark} a **[serious concern]{.mark}**]{.underline}
regarding the deployment of geoengineering8,12,13,14 (but see ref. 15).
Furthermore, the concomitant rapid climate changes16 (Fig. 1) represent
potentially the most significant and yet also unexplored consequences
for biodiversity and ecosystems from geoengineering5.

#### Wrecks the environment

Christopher H. **Trisos** et al., 20**18** -- Research Chair at African
Climate and Development Initiative, Doctorate in Zoology, MSc. In
Integrative Bioscience, BSc. In Ecology and Economics. "Potentially
dangerous consequences for biodiversity of solar geoengineering
implementation and termination," ResearchGate,
<https://www.researchgate.net/publication/322643124_Potentially_dangerous_consequences_for_biodiversity_of_solar_geoengineering_implementation_and_termination>,
DOI:10.1038/s41559-017-0431-0 // Astra4

[As climate changes]{.mark}, the [appropriate conditions
necessary]{.mark} [for]{.mark} the [persistence of a species
move]{.mark} across the Earth, driving species\' geographic range
movements in response to climate change17,18. Indeed, [**[species that
fail to track moving climates]{.mark} may [go extinct]{.mark}**,
[despite]{.mark} [suitable]{.mark} climate [conditions]{.mark} being
[present elsewhere]{.mark}19]{.underline},20. To this end, climate
velocities---the rate of climate change through time divided by the rate
across space21,22---quantify the speeds and directions of shifting
climates and have predicted range movements for a wide variety of marine
and terrestrial species17,18. Climate velocities thus provide a more
biologically relevant metric of exposure to climate change than
traditional macroclimatic anomalies, and can identify regions where
species are threatened by being unable to move fast enough to track
moving climates21, 23,24.

We used a 1° latitude--longitude grid to compare climate velocities for
the Geoengineering Model Intercomparison Project (GeoMIP) G4 scenario25
to representative concentration pathway 4.5 (RCP4.5)26, an intermediate
emissions climate change scenario without geoengineering. We also
compared G4 geoengineering to historical climate data (1960--2014). In
G4, geoengineering begins in 2020 with an injection of 5 Tg
SO2---equivalent to a quarter of the 1991 Pinatubo Mountain volcanic
eruption---into the stratosphere at the Equator, which is then continued
annually until being terminated abruptly in 2070. This results in
approximately equal sulphur injections to the Northern and Southern
Hemispheres. We selected the G4 scenario because it posits that
geoengineering is used only to partially offset increases in radiative
forcing from RCP4.5, in which CO2 emissions peak around 2040, making it
both more credible and conservative than other GeoMIP scenarios25.

[[Global cooling from rapid geoengineering implementation]{.mark} (Fig.
1a,b) **[results in temperature velocity vectors with]{.mark} the
[opposite direction to warming]{.mark}**]{.underline} (that is, negative
velocities) that have especially high speeds [in polar regions]{.mark}
and the [Indo-Pacific]{.mark} Ocean (Fig. 2a). In contrast, the
[Amazon]{.mark} Basin, eastern [Pacific]{.mark}, [Alaska]{.mark} and the
Weddell Sea [have high positive temperature velocities from
warming]{.mark}, in agreement with known patterns of [[increased
probability of]{.mark} El Niño following **[large volcanic
eruptions]{.mark}**]{.underline}27,28. For the many species already
closely tracking positive climate velocities for recent warming17,18
(Fig. 2c), the rapid switch from positive to negative velocities with
geoengineering implementation would be expected to halt or even reverse
climate-driven range shifts. In contrast, where species-range shifts
have lagged past climate warming, the impact of large negative
temperature velocities associated with cooling would be reduced and
could be beneficial29. However, the [strong decadal-scale
climate]{.mark} variability from rapid implementation (Fig. 1) would
[[lead to repeated switching of]{.mark} the [climate signal]{.mark}
(warming--cooling--warming), and **[such variability]{.mark} is [known
from]{.mark} stochastic population [biology]{.mark} theory [to
negatively affect population persistence]{.mark}30**,
**[increasing]{.mark} the risk of local
[extinctions]{.mark}.**]{.underline}

![figure 2](media/image3.jpg){width="6.5in"
height="6.831944444444445in"}

a--d, Temperature velocities. e--h, Precipitation velocities. In c,g,
the historical observations for 1960--2014 are shown (CRU TS3.23 for
land and HadISST 1.1 for oceans). The speed (km yr−1) of shifting
temperature and precipitation isoclines is the same for positive and
negative velocities of equal magnitude. The sign of the velocity
indicates a trend of local warming (+) or cooling (--) for temperature,
and wetting (+) or drying (--) for precipitation. A change of sign
between scenarios represents a 180° change in the direction of climate
movement (for example, from poleward to equatorward or, in mountain
regions, from upslope to downslope).

Taking risks

This would allow for negative emissions of around 1.2bn to 6.3bn tonnes
of carbon, depending on the type of bioenergy that is used, the

[conversion]{.mark} of biomass to hydrogen is [still far from being
