### Marine Geoengineering Fails

#### \[Generic\] Marine-based geoengineering sucks

Center for International Environmental Law (**CIEL**), 20**24** --
International environmental law organization, conducts their own
research on environmental issues and help educate organizations,
corporations and the public. "The Risks of Geoengineering: Accelerating
Biodiversity Loss and Compounding Planetary Crises," Center for
International Environmental Law,
[https://www.ciel.org/reports/risks-of-geoengineering
//](https://www.ciel.org/reports/risks-of-geoengineering%20//) Astra4

As with mineral-based OAE, [enhanced weathering]{.mark} would [require
large-scale mining]{.mark} roughly [equivalent to today\'s global coal
mining industry]{.mark}, which would [exacerbate]{.mark} habitat
degradation and loss and have [deleterious impacts]{.mark} on
freshwater. Also, similarly to OAE, enhanced weathering without
additional mining has been proposed. Using industrial and mine waste to
achieve enhanced weathering on land, like with OAE, comes with
uncertainties about the ecological toxicity impacts of spreading crushed
uncharacterized materials over land-based ecosystems and agricultural
lands.

[Biomass]{.mark}-based CDR technologies would [directly damage marine
and terrestrial ecosystems]{.mark}. Biochar uses pyrolysis to
theoretically sequester carbon from feedstocks, including crop and wood
residues, manure, tires, plastics, municipal waste, and sewage. Each
feedstock [poses]{.mark} [unique ecotoxicity risks through]{.mark} the
[bioaccumulation of toxins]{.mark} taken up by plants grown in
biochar-amended soils or through the inhalation of particles associated
with production and long-term breakdown. A popularly cited study claims
this [technology could]{.mark} theoretically [capture up to 12 percent
of]{.mark} [annual]{.mark} anthropogenic [greenhouse gas
emissions]{.mark}, [but]{.mark} this would [require converting over 170
million hectares]{.mark} of tropical grasslands into biomass
plantations.\" This is roughly the size of Libya, the sixteenth-largest
nation in the world by landmass. In addition to this large-scale,
unsustainable conversion of biodiverse lands, achieving idealized
[deployment]{.mark} would [require]{.mark} [realizing 100
percent]{.mark} [of]{.mark} the [production potential]{.mark} of
abandoned, degraded cropland [for biomass crops]{.mark}, as well as
using 25 percent of all cattle manure and 90 percent of pig and poultry
manure as feedstock.\" [Pyrolysis facilities emit pollution]{.mark} in
the area, which may [negatively impact biodiversity and human
health]{.mark}. The permit application for a [proposed facility
in]{.mark} Saratoga, [New York]{.mark} (USA) that would produce biochar
from sewage sludge exemplifies the emission of such harmful pollutants
in its request to [emit]{.mark} the following toxins: per- and
polyfluoroalkyl substances ([PFAS]{.mark}), naphthalene[, arsenic,
cadmium, lead, mercury, hydrogen fluoride]{.mark}, and particulate
matter.º1

[Implementing]{.mark} large-scale BECCS would [have even greater
land-use implications]{.mark}. [Idealized]{.mark} [BECCS]{.mark}
intended to keep to the Paris Agreement\'s warming limits would [require
cultivating up to 3 billion hectares of productive land]{.mark} -
[roughly]{.mark} equivalent to [twice]{.mark} the [world\'s current
total]{.mark} [cultivated lands]{.mark}.\* Biomass plantations have been
shown to negatively impact biodiversity, including increased exposure to
and decreased resilience to diseases, as well as habitat and food source
loss. These impacts contribute to declines in the richness of plant and
animal species,93 decreasing the resilience of ecosystems due to loss of
genetic diversity. The inevitable sizable carbon emissions from the
large-scale conversion of land for biomass cultivation would lead to
significant loss of natural areas rich in biodiversity as well as
colonize productive crop and grazing lands for carbon sequestration at
the risk of increased food insecurity and loss of ecosystem functions.

### Unknown Consequences

#### Harms the regional climate

Alan **Robock**, 20**08** -- Climatologist, Distinguished Professor,
Ph.D. in Meteorology, School of Environmental and Biological Science,
Associate Director of Center for Environmental Prediction, Supported by
the National Science Foundation, Director of Meteorology. "20 Reasons
Why Geoengineering May Be a Bad Idea," Bulletin of the Atomic
Scientists, Vol 64, No. 2 , p14-18, 59,
https://climate.envsci.rutgers.edu/pdf/20Reasons.pdf, Rutgers University
// Astra4

Geo-engineering proponents often suggest that volcanic eruptions are an
innocuous natural analog for stratospheric injection of sulfate
aerosols. The 1991 [eruption of Mount Pinatubo]{.mark} on the Philippine
island of Luzon, which injected 20 megatons of sulfur dioxide gas into
the stratosphere, [[produced a sulfate aerosol cloud]{.mark} that is
[said to have caused global cooling]{.mark} for a couple of years
without adverse effects.]{.underline} **[[However]{.mark}, [researchers
at]{.mark} the [National Center for Atmospheric Research showed]{.mark}
in 2007 that the Pinatubo [eruption caused large hydrological
responses]{.mark},]{.underline}** including reduced precipitation, soil
moisture, and river flow in many regions.5 [Simulations of the climate
response to volcanic eruptions have also shown [large impacts on
regional climate]{.mark},]{.underline} but whether these are good
analogs for the geoengineering response requires further investigation.

Scientists have also [seen]{.mark} volcanic eruptions in the tropics
produce [[changes in atmospheric circulation]{.mark}, [causing **winter
warming**]{.mark} over continents in the Northern Hemisphere, as well as
eruptions at high latitudes weaken the Asian and African monsoons,
causing **[reduced precipitation]{.mark}**.6 In fact, the
eight-month-long eruption of the Laki fissure in Iceland in 1783--1784
contributed to **[famine]{.mark}** in Africa, India, and
Japan.]{.underline}

If scientists and engineers were able to inject smaller amounts of
stratospheric aerosols than result from volcanic eruptions, how would
they affect summer wind and precipitation patterns? Could attempts to
geoengineer isolated regions (say, the Arctic) be confined there?
Scientists need to investigate these scenarios. [[At]{.mark} the fall
2007 [American Geophysical Union meeting]{.mark}, researchers presented
preliminary findings from **[several]{.mark} different [climate
model]{.mark}**[s]{.mark} that simulated geoengineering schemes and
[found]{.mark} that [they reduced precipitation over wide
regions]{.mark}]{.underline}[,]{.mark} **[[condemning hundreds of
millions of people to drought]{.mark}.]{.underline}**

[If]{.mark} humans [adopt]{.mark}ed [geoengineering as]{.mark} a

carbon emissions, the **[[ocean]{.mark} would [continue to become more
acidic]{.mark}]{.underline}**, because about half of all excess carbon
dioxide in the atmosphere is removed by ocean uptake. The ocean is
[[already **30 percent more acidic** than]{.mark} it was [before]{.mark}
the [Industrial Revolution]{.mark}]{.underline}, and [[continued]{.mark}
[acidification **threatens**]{.mark} **the [entire oceanic biological
chain]{.mark}**, from [coral reefs]{.mark} right up [to
humans]{.mark}]{.underline}.

#### Advancing geoengineering would undermine long-term climate justice

The legitimizing of solar geoengineering is emerging as a major threat
to climate justice.\
[Solar geoengineering is the ultimate "technical fix]{.underline}"
\[4.17\] \[4.18\] \[4.19\]; it [does nothing to\

understated]{.underline} \[4.13\]. [[Solar

[mechanism for powerful elites to manipulate]{.mark} the Earth's

[this [non- transformative tech]{.mark}]{.underline}nology [[could

#### No international geoengineering governance

Jennie **Stephens et al. 23**, Professor of Sustainable Science and
Policy at Northeastern University, bachelor's degree in Environmental
Science and Public Policy from Harvard, PhD in Environmental Science and
Engineering from the California Institute of Technology. Frank Biermann
is a German political scientist at Utrecht University and founder of the
Earth System Governance Project. Raymond Pierrehumbert is a Professor of
Planetary Physics at the University of Oxford. Aarti Gupta is a
Professor in Computer Science at Princeton University. Dhanasree Jayaram
is a Non-Resident Fellow at the Centre on Climate Politics and Security
at the Finnish Institute of International Affairs. ("Solar
Geoengineering Myths Debunked", Solar Geoengineering Non-Use Agreement,
[[https://www.solargeoeng.org/wp-content/uploads/2023/02/SGNUA_1_Briefing_note.pdf]{.underline}](https://www.solargeoeng.org/wp-content/uploads/2023/02/SGNUA_1_Briefing_note.pdf))

The [[world lacks]{.mark} a [system of global governance that
could]{.mark}]{.underline} equitably and [democratically]{.underline}
regulate and [[manage deployment of]{.mark} solar [geoengineering at
planetary scale. Long-term stable international governance would be
needed given]{.mark}]{.underline} the [time-frame of [solar
geoengineering]{.mark}]{.underline} deployment that would [[range from
decades to]{.underline}]{.mark} many [[centuries]{.underline}]{.mark}.
[Yet [recent geopolitical crises]{.mark} [suggest growing
instability]{.mark}, worsening ineffectiveness and expanding inequities
in global governance.]{.underline} This has led hundreds of scholars of
global governance and environmental policy to call for an International
Non-Use Agreement on Solar Geoengineering. [[Increasing]{.mark}
[superpower hostilities make future collaborative governance of solar

dev]{.mark}elopment among powerful countries [could lead to an arms

interests -- at the cost of small and less powerful nations.

#### Global geoengineering governance is impossible in the current geopolitical climate

Frank **Biermann et al. 21**, professor of global sustainability
governance, recipient of the Volvo Environmental Prize and the
Distinguished Scholar Award in Environmental Studies, has a PhD in
political science from the Free University of Berlin and a master's
degree in international law from the University of Aberdeen. ("Solar
geoengineering: The case for an international non-use agreement," WIREs
Climate Change,
[[https://www.solargeoeng.org/wp-content/uploads/2023/05/WIREs-Climate-Change-2022-Solar-geoengineering-The-case-for-an-international-nonuse-agreement-1.pdf]{.underline}](https://www.solargeoeng.org/wp-content/uploads/2023/05/WIREs-Climate-Change-2022-Solar-geoengineering-The-case-for-an-international-nonuse-agreement-1.pdf))

Our main concern, however, is with the global governance challenges
posed by the confluence of technical, political, and ethical risks of
such large-scale interventions at planetary scale. [We see the
deployment of solar geoengineering as impossible to govern fairly and
effectively in the current international political system, under
assumptions of effective global participation, inclusiveness, and
justice]{.underline}. Other critics have argued in the past that solar
geoengineering is "undesirable, ungovernable, and unattainable" (Hulme,
2014) and incompatible with democratic decision-making (Stephens et al.,
2021; Szerszynski et al., 2013). We share these worries and turn them
into a concrete policy proposal.

Our core concern as governance scholars is that [solar geoengineering at
planetary scale would require complex global decisions on the places and
manner of deployment, the intensity]{.underline} of deployment (i.e.,
the degree of cooling), [the duration]{.underline} of deployment, [and
the responsibility and compensation for any harm that may be
caused]{.underline} (Jinnah et al., 2019). As solar geoengineering would
impact all countries, fair and just governance would require the
effective control over the deployment of such technologies by all
countries (Holahan & Kashwan, 2019). Importantly, [the type and degree
of deployment would affect different countries differently, and risks
would be unevenly spread]{.underline}. As the specific manner of
deployment would influence the distribution of these risks, [democratic
decision-making at planetary scale would be even more crucial---and even
harder to safeguard in a fair and just manner.]{.underline}

[Globally inclusive decision-making and political control over solar
geoengineering would be especially important for the]{.underline}
poorest and [most vulnerable countries.]{.underline} Six-hundred and
ninety-six million people live in extreme poverty, on less than USD 1.90
a day, and 3.2 billion people live on less than USD 5.50 a day (Aguilar
et al., 2021). Over 820 million people suffer from hunger, which is
equal to one in every nine people worldwide (UN Food and Agriculture
Organization, 2019). [These global poor are extremely vulnerable to any
change in their environment and threatened the most by any
risks]{.underline} or side effects that might result from the deployment
of solar geoengineering at planetary scale. Conversely, the global poor
would also be the first to suffer from drastic climate change. Various
[researchers have argued that this suffering could be alleviated by
solar geoengineering]{.underline} (Harding et al., 2020; Wagner, 2021),
leading some to postulate a moral obligation of industrialized countries
to engage in solar geoengineering research to compensate for past and
current greenhouse gas emissions (Svoboda et al., 2018). [Yet one cannot
achieve climate justice by addressing one aspect of justice and
violating another]{.underline} (Schlosberg & Collins, 2014). [While
historical responsibility]{.underline} for the inequitable impacts of
climate change [is an important demand of climate justice, so is
inclusion and participation in planetary-scale decisions.]{.underline}

Both the all-affected principle of democracy and procedural justice
require governance that is globally inclusive. [Because of the high
vulnerability of the least developed countries and many other countries
in the Global South, their governments would need]{.underline} to have
[decisive control ove]{.underline}r whether and [how to
deploy]{.underline} solar geoengineering technologies. [Yet there is
little evidence to suggest]{.underline} that [countries]{.underline}
most [able to develop tech]{.underline}nologies for solar geoengineering
[would be willing to transfer effective control of such geopolitically
important tech]{.underline}nologies [to the most vulnerable countries in
the Global South]{.underline}. Considering the stakes for the Global
South, mere consultation of least developed and other developing
countries over technology development and potential deployment---as
suggested by some proponents of solar geoengineering---would not be
sufficient. [Full knowledge integration for the Global South is
important]{.underline}, for sure (Rahman et al., 2018; Winickoff et al.,
2015). [But eventually, it is effective and enforceable political
control by the Global South that would be required.]{.underline}

To guarantee such globally inclusive and effective governance,
[countries powerful enough]{.underline} to develop and deploy
technologies for solar geoengineering w[ould need to place their
tech]{.underline}nologies [under the control of]{.underline} effective
multilateral [institutions, with guarantees of collective veto rights
for the most vulnerable nations.]{.underline} [The current world order
seems unfit to reach such far-reaching agreements]{.underline} on fair
and effective political control over solar geoengineering deployment.
The United Nations General Assembly, as the highest organ of the world
organization, declared climate change in 1988 a "common concern of
humankind", but the assembly lacks enforcement powers and its decisions
are not binding. The United Nations Environment Programme (UNEP) with
its United Nations Environment Assembly, which deals with many
environmental issues, is constrained in its mandate and operational
capacity. In 2019, a proposed UNEP resolution to undertake a cautionary
assessment of geoengineering options, including solar geoengineering,
was blocked by the United States, Brazil, and Saudi Arabia (Chemnick,
2019). Like the United Nations General Assembly, the United Nations
Framework Convention on Climate Change also lacks the institutional
force that could guarantee just, equitable and effective multilateral
control over deployment of solar geoengineering technologies at
planetary scale.

In addition, [any global decisions on the details of the
deployment]{.underline} of solar geoengineering [are unlikely to find
consensus. Disagreements about]{.underline} some
[parameters]{.underline}---for example, the degree of cooling, the
duration of deployment, or the specific latitudes and distribution of
aerosols---[will inevitably occur. Such situations would require clear
and reliable decision-making procedures for solving these
disagreements.]{.underline} Most United Nations bodies follow the
principle of sovereign equality, which grants each country one vote. It
is unlikely that technologically advanced nations such as the United
States or Russia would accept such a system of oversight for
planetary-scale use of solar geoengineering. [Universally accepted
alternatives to one-country-one-vote decision-making]{.underline},
however, [are not in sight]{.underline}.

Importantly, [any decision by a global body]{.underline} to deploy solar
geoengineering technologies [would require enforcement power in case
some countries disagree with a majority decision and threaten
counteractions.]{.underline} The literature even discusses scenarios of
counter-geoengineering by countries that disagree with the solar
geoengineering programs of other countries (Heyen et al., 2019). [The
U]{.underline}nited [N]{.underline}ations [S]{.underline}ecurity
[C]{.underline}ouncil [has a mandate to act]{.underline} if it deems a
situation to be a threat to international peace and security. [Yet the
Security Council]{.underline}, with five countries having permanent
seats and veto rights, [does not enjoy the global legitimacy to
effectively regulate future global deployment of solar geoengineering
technologies. And it cannot take actions against any of its five
permanent members]{.underline} (i.e., China, France, Russia, the United
Kingdom, and the United States).

These concerns about formal governance also arise with more informal
governance arrangements, such as multi-stake- holder dialogues or
voluntary codes of conduct (Jinnah et al., 2019). Such arrangements face
similar barriers to entry by less powerful actors, and they risk
contributing to premature legitimization of these speculative
technologies as well (Conca, 2019). Science networks in general are
heavily biased towards a few industrialized countries, with less
economically powerful countries having little or no direct control over
them (Biermann & Möller, 2019; Stephens et al., 2021). Moreover,
[technocratic governance based on expert commissions or scientific
modeling of distributive consequences cannot adjudicate complex global
conflicts over values, risk allocation, and differences in risk
acceptance]{.underline} (Flegal & Gupta, 2018).

In short, [the deployment of solar geoengineering at planetary scale
would require entirely new international organizations with convincing
means of democratic control and unprecedented enforcement powers. Such
organizations do not exist.]{.underline}

[Without effective]{.underline} global and democratic
[controls]{.underline}, however, [the geopolitics of possible unilateral
deployment]{.underline} of solar geoengineering [would be complex and
frightening.]{.underline} International law remains vague when it comes
to development and deployment of such technologies (Reynolds, 2019).
[In]{.underline} the [absence of effective governance mechanisms, expert
debates]{.underline} and scientific research assessments [within a few
industrialized countries could develop into a form]{.underline} of "de
facto governance" (Gupta & Möller, 2019), [but]{.underline} often in
ways [not legitimized]{.underline} or supported [by developing countries
in particular]{.underline} (Biermann & Möller, 2019). Given the
anticipated low monetary costs of some of these technologies, such as
stratospheric aerosols injection, a few countries could engage in solar
geoengineering unilaterally or in small coalitions even when other
countries oppose such deployment---a possibility economists have
presented as the "free-driver effect" (Wagner & Weitzman, 2012). Some
proponents from the Global North see it even explicitly as an advantage
of solar geoengineering that it could be deployed "without broad
international cooperation" (Reynolds, 2021).

Last but not least, [speculative hopes about]{.underline} [the future
availability of solar geoengineering]{.underline} technologies could
[threaten commitments to mitigation and reduce incentives for
governments, businesses, and societies to do their utmost to achieve
decarbonization]{.underline} or carbon neutrality as soon as possible
(Asayama et al., 2019). Powerful industry interests, notably from the
energy sector, have long invested in delaying stringent climate
policies; seeking out technical alternatives such as carbon dioxide
removal and sequestration (Carton et al., 2020; Lamb et al., 2020; Low &
Boettcher, 2020; McLaren & Markusson, 2020); or denying the phenomenon
of climate change altogether (Oreskes & Conway, 2010). The [looming
possibility of future solar geoengineering could become a powerful
argument for]{.underline} energy companies and [oil-dependent countries
to further delay decarbonization policies. This risk is particularly
high now]{.underline}, with a surge of countries announcing their
intention to reach net-zero emissions by 2050 or earlier.

both countries are facing.\]{.underline}
　[Prime Minister Ishiba reiterated Japan's unwavering commitment to
fundamentally reinforcing its defense capabilities and President Trump
welcomed such commitment. President Trump underscored the unwavering
commitment by the U.S. to the defense of Japan, using its full range of
capabilities, including nuclear.]{.underline} The two leaders once again

　[[The]{.mark} two [leaders]{.mark} then [concurred to elevate the

## Data Centers Adv

### AT AI/Blockchain

#### NO Singularity -- infinite feedback loops are impossible (laws of thermodynamics) and at minimum take centuries. AI already reached its plateau. 

Craig Woods 2024, (served as the Director of Infectious Disease &
Biosecurity Projects for Arizona State University\'s Complex Adaptive
Systems Initiative (CASI)), "The AI singularity: Really, guys?", Aug 23,
2024, Medium,
https://medium.com/%40hindmost99/the-ai-singularity-really-guys-5012ee5cecff

[[This is all]{.mark} quite sexy and cool to imagine --- and
[absurd]{.mark}. I mean --- [physically impossible (not merely
implausible]{.mark}), [just as dumb as a perpetual motion machine or
time travel.]{.mark} Fun to imagine for science fiction stories, but not
something to plan for, or try to create. And, more than that, given the
real world 'physics' of feedback loops --- potentially catastrophic to
attempt.]{.underline}

Where does it come from, this concept of a singularity? Simple
extrapolation of historical trends. It took hundreds of thousands of
years for humanity to move from tool-less existence to the age of simple
tool use, tens of thousands to move from simple tools to early
industrial age, only a century to reach the information age, and now
mere decades to begin to see the glimmers of machine intelligence
capable of reproducing human-level cognitive tasks. Why wouldn't this
trend of accelerating evolution continue, following the exponential
curve up ever faster?

[The simple answer is 'the physics of complex systems', but given the
extraordinary complexity of these ideas --- of the definition and
function of intelligence, of the social, political and economic systems
required to develop intelligence --- it isn't simple to elaborate. And
the physics involved --- 'non-linear systems dynamics', '[complex
systems theory',]{.mark} 'chaos theory' --- [isn't quite]{.mark} the
[clear cut]{.mark}-and-dried kind of [physics]{.mark} like Newtons laws,
relativity or even quantum mechanics(!). We are still struggling as a
species to elucidate and internalize the constraints that arise from
complexity and feedback loops --- we don't even have a good name for the
subtle, not-expressed-as-equations-but-nonetheless-binding rules that we
have discovered about systems of things. But [those rules are real and
binding]{.mark}, and mean that the [exponential growth of
intelligence]{.mark}, biological or machine, that arguably started back
in pre-historic times [will stop before]{.mark} intelligent machines
trigger a true '[singularity]{.mark}'.]{.underline}

Limits to growth

First, the simple stuff:

[Feedback [loops always stop]{.mark} themselves. They [either exhaust
their supply of]{.mark} one or more [inputs]{.mark} to the loop, [or
they destabilize their environment]{.mark} or infrastructure they rely
on for growth so that growth stops, and often collapses.]{.underline}

Another way to say this is that there are always 'limits to growth'.
This is a rule with a fraught (and increasingly poignant) history, first
elucidated in popular form in the book of the same title back in the
1970's. A group of graduate students used the primitive computing
infrastructure of the 1970's to define a set of abstract models of a
global social-political-economic system, with a set of interdependencies
and feedbacks that were a crude but useful representation of the real
world. They then ran simulations of hundreds of years, using a variety
of parameters, to see how the systems evolved. And they observed that
the system nearly always sustained growth --- sometimes exponentially
--- but also that the growth always eventually leveled off, and often
'crashed'. All but 2 of the scenarios that they modeled showed continued
exponential growth for a time, but then the growth didn't just stop, the
models always showed the population crashing back to levels much lower
than the starting point of the simulation. As far as they could see,
unless civilization preemptively aborted the exponential growth by
'voluntarily' adopting strict limits on growth, the growth would stop
catastrophically, with a great 'die back' of humanity. And using the
models, they were able to see that the reason for this inevitable end to
exponential growth, the reason that there were never true
'singularities', was the simple statement above. [A feedback
[loop]{.mark} [requires inputs]{.mark} (that's the Second Law of
Thermodynamics), [and]{.mark} it [produces]{.mark} side effects or
'[waste]{.mark}' outputs (also the [Second Law of
Thermodynamics]{.mark}). If the growth becomes exponential, [it requires
exponentially increasing inputs to be sustained, and it produces
exponentially increasing waste]{.mark} streams as a consequence of its
loop. At some point, either [the loop exhausts]{.mark} one or more
[sources, or]{.mark} it [saturates]{.mark} one or more ['sinks' that are
absorbing the wastes]{.mark}. Either of those will cause the growth to
cease, and often trigger the collapse]{.underline}.

We're all familiar now with the logic of global climate change --- the
increasing generation of carbon dioxide (a waste output of combustion),
and chemical synthesis, and even food production, since all fertilizer
is produced from natural gas synthesis, which emits carbon dioxide as a
waste product is collecting in the atmosphere (and in the oceans),
causing increased global temperatures (and ocean acidification). And if
we were to continue on the exponential growth rates of the past few
centuries, both in population and in the intensity of our energy use per
capita, modelling suggests that we could make the earth far less
habitable --- less land in climates suitable for agriculture, meaning
less food, meaning fewer people (and also potentially far less ocean
life due to the acidification and warming, meaning a lot less food...)

That's the canonical example of a complex system with feedback loops
(industrial society undergoing exponential population growth and
industrialization) poisoning the environment in a way that will stop the
exponential growth. (Side note: the climate models used for the early
climate change modeling were refined and extended versions of the models
that were used in the original Limits to Growth studies). But it is also
an example of the difficulty of both formulating the 'physical laws' of
complex systems in a compelling way: the original Limits to Growth book
was widely mocked and dismissed, and even with enormous investments in
validating the climate modeling that shows the dire consequences of the
carbon dioxide emissions, and with the dire record-annihilating
temperatures we have observed, the global consensus that there truly are
fundamental limits to growth is only weakly held, and still routinely
attacked or undermined. People, especially people with vested interests,
don't want to accept that humanity must accept limits, and don't
acknowledge the existence of such ephemeral constraints as "there are
limits to growth". And so, society as a whole continues to increase our
emissions, and undermines the attempts to reduce carbon emissions at
every turn.

Or, in the 'techo-utopian' branch of thought, the very very smart people
say 'technology will solve that', or specifically 'after the
singularity, none of that will matter any more, because we won't pollute
any more, and we won't need to consume (food \| water \| air )'. And,
they continue, it doesn't have to go on forever --- just long enough to
get to the point where the intelligence is smart enough to mitigate the
side effects, and develop adaptations to maintain the supplies needed to
continue the growth. And once we are no longer bound by biology or
energy constraints or those pesky ineffable laws like 'there are limits
to growth', well, we will attain a literal heaven on earth.

'Hidden (more like 'Ignored') Assumptions:

[There are a [TON of hidden assumptions]{.mark} in the concept of the
'machine intelligence singularity' that are necessary to make such a
scenario possible. I'm pretty sure this is not even close to a complete
list, but it doesn't have to be --- [even]{.mark} these [basic
assumptions make a singularity]{.mark} either unlikely or
[impossible]{.mark}:]{.underline}

a\) That there even is such a thing as a machine intelligence that is
'smarter' than humans, specifically at designing intelligence, to start
the feedback loop. This is one of the more reasonable assumptions to
make. We already use a variety of tools to assist in chip design, for
example, and we are seeing that generative AI can take an English
specification and produce (mostly) functional code. But it is still a
huge leap to say we can build a system that will be able to design a
'better brain'. What does 'smarter' mean, though? [So far most of the
[machine]{.mark} intelligence we can build [does a small subset of what
humans do]{.mark}, but faster and at a larger scale, or for generative
AI --- [it merely mimics the patterns]{.mark} that it has observed in
human communications. [Neither]{.mark} of those seem likely to [produce
truly innovative]{.mark} or ground breaking 'new' [intelligence]{.mark}
that can solve problems that humans cannot solve.]{.underline} I have a
separate essay in mind to discuss exactly how (and whether) AI can truly
help humanity --- what problems do we need it to solve, and how likely
it seems that it will solve them. For now, let's just say I'm deeply
skeptical that machine intelligence will be able to do much to help
humanity with our most critical challenges --- and is far more likely to
exacerbate them.

b\) That even if we manage to build an AI that is smarter than a single
human it will be smart enough to trigger the feedback loop. This is the
wrong bar, clearly. Humanity is advancing in our design of machine
intelligence collectively, leveraging the knowledge and efforts of tens
of thousands of humans with specialized knowledge and skills. In the
short term, AI will augment the rate of advance without reaching the
goal of being smarter than any one human. [And being smarter than a
single human won't be a step-change in capabilities --- we [will]{.mark}
[need an intelligence that is smarter than humanity
collectively]{.mark}. That is a [threshold]{.mark} that [is several
orders of magnitude harder than being smarter than a single
human.]{.mark}]{.underline}

c\) That intelligence is somehow an unlimited domain, or space. That
simply by increasing the number of 'nodes', or 'neurons', or by applying
ever more sophisticated algorithms, you can solve ever harder problems.
This is inferred by extrapolating from animals to humans --- look at the
great leap that was made when humans evolved our sophisticated
cognition, and then at the accelerating pace of technological evolution
we have demonstrated over the past 200 years (for example). Surely, this
is exponential growth, and we can extrapolate that there is still room
to go --- greater heights to scale, bigger brains to evolve, right?

This is not a gimmee, though. [[All complex systems]{.mark}, organic or
mechanical, [are subject to diminishing returns]{.mark} where after a
certain point, just making things bigger, or [adding more inputs makes
less and less difference]{.mark}, until you reach a physical
limit]{.underline}. [It is very plausible, and possibly the null
hypothesis that, at the very least, ever greater intelligences [will
require exponentially greater inputs --- energy, space, cooling, data,
training time]{.mark} --- to continue to increase. And there may be a
plateau in what can be achieved, or the size and complexity of problems
that can be solved purely by building bigger and better intelligence.
The limits imposed by [physical laws don't change]{.mark} (much) [based
on how intelligent you are]{.mark} --- [they]{.mark} are physical
limits, that [always apply.]{.mark}]{.underline}

However, it is reasonable to believe that humanity itself is not the
'apex' of what is possible --- we evolved to the point where we could
outcompete other mammals in our ecological niches, not to reach the
highest form of possible cognition. So, lets give this to the
Singularity buffs. We'll assume that there are better intelligences that
are possible. But we have to keep in mind those diminishing returns, and
escalating costs. And, that the scaling of intelligence may reach limits
beyond which we simply cannot afford to build and operate the greater
intelligences, even utilizing whatever improvements the better
intelligences provide.

We have certainly lived through very exciting times, where computing has
completely reconfigured our world in just a few short decades, and it
shows no signs of slowing down, especially with the advent of generative
AI. You can say that we are already in the singularity, in a way, our
lives already nearly unrecognizable to people who lived a mere two
hundred years ago. [Except, [we]{.mark} do [see signs of slowing
down]{.mark} of the ever increasing power of computation to refashion
our world. Moore's law (or the more salient "more-than-Moore's" law) has
slowed down in the last few years; Generative [AI improvements are
seeing a tail-off in the rate at which larger training sets produce
improved results.]{.mark}]{.underline}

d\) that there will not be any limits that slow or stop the exponential
feedback. This is a very bad assumption, a 'non-physical' assumption.
Exponential growth from feedback loops always slows and stops. Either
the growth exhausts the resources that feed the loop, or the growth
destabilizes the system on which the growth depends. Even nuclear
fission, the most potent and primal runaway feedback loop stops when it
consumes all of the available fissionable material, or the material is
scattered by the massive energy emissions of the fission reaction.
Population explosions are limited by exhaustion of food supplies, fire
burns through the available fuel --- every system has limits that
eventually moderate and stall the exponential growth. The one actual
example of limitless exponential growth (in some sense) is an actual
gravitational singularity --- the progressive accretion of mass in a
small enough region that it collapses space/time into an infinitely
dense point. This is the metaphorical model of the machine intelligence
feedback loop, but it is an inapt comparison. Unlike gravitational
singularities, machine intelligence requires (likely exponentially
increasing amounts of) energy, cooling, space, data, money, etc to keep
growing exponentially (even assuming it is possible). And there are
other limits that seem like they will plausibly derail the exponential
growth: poisoning the 'environment' it needs to sustain the investments
in the exponential growth. Here, the environment is likely the entire
world political and economic system. AI is very likely to have
profoundly destabilizing effects on the world socio-political system and
economy. Will the world be able to maintain the ever-escalating costs
needed to sustain an exponential feedback loop in the face of this
dislocation? That is a huge unknown --- we don't even have a good
understanding of how the primitive machine intelligences we have today
are going to impact our world, beyond the evident destructive power they
have on social consensus building and their utility in fostering
autocratic governance models in the hands of oligarchs. Durable mass
unemployment seems to be a highly likely very near term consequence of
even the crude generative AI that we have now --- we've probably already
seen the first small wave of job destruction in the 2023--2024 layoffs
in high tech, and tech companies are internally telling their software
engineers to 'learn a different skill set' because they anticipate
near-total automation of software coding in the very near future.
(Generating code for computer software is very 'easy' for generative AI,
because the rules for the language are much simpler than human
communication, and because there is a ton of 'reuse' in software, giving
the model very clear patterns to learn from. Boring and unoriginal code
is perfectly okay, lowering the bar still further for AI to replace and
economically dislocate software engineers.)

e\) Speed --- one of the implicit assumptions in the singularity lore is
that once the feedback loop is triggered, it will be a matter of a few
years, then months and days before the machine intelligences are
sophisticated enough to re-work human existence. Each new generation of
machine intelligence will create the next in shorter and shorter
windows. This is a completely unrealistic model. As noted above the
systems will require power, cooling, networking, water, and training
time. [If [new]{.mark} generations of computing [hardware are
required]{.mark} it might take decades to re-tool the supply chains,
starting with [new chip fab equipment]{.mark}, then [new fabs]{.mark},
then [deploying the new chips]{.mark} in systems to data centers with
requisite infrastructure to run them. If the assumption of near-zero
turnaround for successive generations relies on full automation of the
entire economy --- well, that will be profoundly destabilizing to the
socio-political system as noted above, and [will take]{.mark} decades
(or [centuries) by itself]{.mark}. It will also require [vast amounts of
raw materials, and the requisite supply chains]{.mark}.]{.underline}

Lets look at how industries advance --- take the automotive industry as
an example. A relatively simple, yet profound change: going from
internal combustion engines to battery-electric power trains (cars are
far simpler to build than computer chips, systems, and networks ). How
long did it take for established auto makers to re-tool to begin
building EV's? A: we don't know yet, but it is more than a decade so
far, and we are only just seeing a small number of automakers reaching
what one might call 'competitive' BEV offerings. Tesla took 13 years to
develop the Model 3, arguably the first mass produced EV. And then what
has happened after that? Tesla built highly automated factories (and
then had to dial it back somewhat when the over-automation actually
reduced the productivity of the assembly lines). But now Tesla is
'stuck' with an assembly line tailored to build the 2 major models that
they now how to produce, and they (apparently) can't update or upgrade
their designs easily. And here we see the frustrating limits of the
'limits to growth' rule. None of the above feels like 'proof'. None of
these observations can be expressed in a way that says "the singularity
will not happen", or "the rate of advancement of machine intelligence
will stop by such and such a date, or due to this specific constraint."
In part that is because I am by no means a professional systems modeler
or analyst, and can't even begin to design a specific model to explore
the possible limits that will apply to the development of machine
intelligence. But it is also the nature of the subtlety of the rules for
complex systems. One of the other rules we've learned about complex
systems is that forecasting and predicting the specific 'trajectory' or
path of a sufficiently complex system is impossible beyond a certain
limit --- like trying to predict the weather for a city on a specific
day more than 10 days in advance. There are simply too many moving
parts, too many interactions and too many subtleties that all add up
over time to blur our ability to predict the future. If we build
simplified models with representative feedbacks and interactions, we can
see patterns, or trends, and even potential 'tipping points', but not
with precision. We can know they are there, but not when, or even if, we
will trigger them. And so it is with the machine intelligence
singularity. We can say with confidence that it will hit limits that
will stop the exponential growth, but not specifically which limits, or
when they will take effect --- or how we will react and adapt when we do
hit the limits. It's frustratingly vague, and to some that means that
the rules are useless, or even that the rules do not exist. But they do.
We are bound by them just as surely as we feel the pull of gravity,
though there effects are far more subtle, and, sorry to overuse the
word, complex. The big, maybe only takeaway here is --- don't plan on a
technological 'singularity' to render all of the current crises we are
dealing with moot. We need to deal with climate change, both by
mitigating it through reduced emissions, and through adaptations like
coping with climate migrations elegantly. Even with that our growth as a
species and a civilization will face ongoing challenges --- fresh water
supply, food production, social stability, ecosystem destruction --- any
one of which could still cause a collapse. We can only escape the limits
to growth conundrum by not growing, either in sheer numbers, or in the
intensity of our resource consumption. Global population will stop
growing and begin declining 'soon', based on current population trends,
but the intensity of our resource consumption is going to keep going up,
resulting in greater impacts on the environment indefinitely.

**[[There are no magic bullets, there will be no 'machine intelligence
savior' coming to save us]{.mark}.]{.underline}**

#### Nuclear energy solves Data centers

**Global Times**, 6-4-20**25**, (is a tabloid publishing in English
since 2009)\"'US-China AI arms race' is a misleading narrative,\" Global
times, https://www.globaltimes.cn/page/202506/1335412.shtml?

[Recently, the [US government set out aims to quadruple US nuclear
capacity by 2050]{.mark}. While some Americans believe that nuclear is
the key to winning the AI race, they began to hype that the US
government\'s move will help the US maintain its early AI advantage in
its competition with China. \"[We\'ve got enough electricity to win the
AI arms race]{.mark} with China,\" [Interior Secretary]{.mark} Doug
Burgum [said]{.mark}.]{.underline} The Global Times invited three
Chinese experts to discuss this US policy and the logic behind
it[.]{.underline}

The US government has signed a series of executive orders to hasten the
deployment of new nuclear power reactors. On the surface, this policy
reflects Washington\'s emphasis on revitalizing American manufacturing,
energy security and related technological progress, but the China factor
behind it should cause deep thought. At present, Washington\'s excessive
emphasis on the competition with China and exaggeration of China\'s
economic and technological \"threats\" may not be conducive to the
realization of its goals in the end.

As an efficient and low-carbon form of energy, nuclear energy plays an
important role in the global energy transition[. The [US has the largest
number of nuclear power reactors]{.mark} in the world, with more than 90
in existence, but the [development]{.mark} of nuclear power has [slowed
down]{.mark} in the past few decades [due to]{.mark} high costs, strict
[regulations]{.mark} and public concerns.]{.underline} [[The]{.mark} new
US government\'s [e]{.mark}xecutive [o]{.mark}rders aim to revitalize
the nuclear power industry by [simplify]{.mark}ing [approval processes,
providing financial support and promoting]{.mark} small modular reactor
([SMR]{.mark}) technology]{.underline}. [This move aims not only to meet
domestic energy needs, but is also closely related to the rapid
development of AI. According to the International Energy Agency,
estimated global [data center]{.mark} electricity [consumption]{.mark}
in 2022 [was]{.mark} around [1]{.mark} to 1.3 [percent]{.mark} [of
global]{.mark} final [electricity]{.mark} demand, and it is expected to
double by 2030. [Nuclear power, with its high energy density and low
carbon characteristics, has become an ideal option to meet this
demand.]{.mark}]{.underline}

#### Nuclear power solves -- high capacity and energy density

Kate Hardin 2025, (holds an MA from Yale University and an MBA from the
Yale School of Management, worked in the energy sector for over 20
years), "Nuclear energy's role in powering data center growth", 09 April
2025, Deloitte Research Center for Energy & Industrials,
https://www.deloitte.com/us/en/insights/industry/power-and-utilities/nuclear-energy-powering-data-centers.html

The nuclear advantage

[[Nuclear energy presents a]{.mark} potential [solution for]{.mark}
meeting some of the growing electricity demands of [data
centers]{.mark}, with its reliable and clean energy profile. [It
provided over 19% of the United States' electricity in 2024]{.mark},
despite [representing less than 8% of the nation's total operating
capacit]{.mark}y.2 Some potential advantages include:]{.underline}

[Reliable baseload power: Nuclear power plants provide firm baseload
power, operating 24/7 regardless of weather conditions. Their
[capacity]{.mark} factor, [exceeding 92.5%,]{.mark} [outperforms]{.mark}
other sources like [natural gas]{.mark} (56%) and renewables like wind
(35%) and solar (25%).3 This [reliability is important for data
centers]{.mark}, helping to ensure uninterrupted operations and maximize
return on investments, especially for AI and generative AI
applications.]{.underline}

[High energy density: Nuclear fuel boasts a [high energy
density]{.mark}. [A small amount of nuclear fuel can generate a vast
amount of electricity]{.mark}, [minimizing fuel storage requirements and
reducing transportation needs]{.mark}. This efficiency can translate to
a smaller physical footprint and enhanced sustainability.]{.underline}

[Scalable power output: A [single]{.mark} nuclear [reactor]{.mark}
typically [generates 800 megawatts]{.mark} (MW) or more of electricity,
readily [meeting the power demands of even the largest data centers (50
MW to 100 MW]{.mark}) and the burgeoning requirements of AI-focused
facilities (up to 5,000 MW).4]{.underline}

Low-carbon emissions: Nuclear power is a clean energy source that
produces virtually no greenhouse gas emissions during operation.5

[Enhanced land use efficiency: Nuclear [power plants require relatively
small land areas]{.mark} compared to other energy sources.6 This
efficiency [is important for data center colocation and can help to
optimize land use]{.mark} and minimize environmental
impact.]{.underline}

https://wciom.com/press-release/russia-and-its-resource-wealth

More than half of Russians know that Russia is at the top of the list of
countries with the most natural resources (52%). Another 41% say that
Russia is in top ten richest countries of the world.

Those who ascribe the leadership to Russia are largely Russians aged
35-44 (58%), as well as respondents aged 45-59 and 60 and over (56% and
52% respectively), Russians aged 25-34 (49%). Young respondents aged
18-24 are less likely to recognize Russia's leadership in this issue
(35%).

[[Information concerning the Russia's resource wealth evokes positive
emotions in Russians]{.underline}]{.mark} (44%). Twenty-five percent of
Russians are indifferent; less than a quarter of Russians say they have
negative emotions about that (24%).

[The [top]{.mark} five [exports Russians are proud of are]{.mark} oil
(33%), **[gas (30%),]{.mark}**]{.underline} timber (15%), military

#### Putin uses gas wealth to frame himself as the [grantor of Russia's prosperity]{.underline}, gas is [central]{.underline} to the [regime's legitimacy]{.underline}.

Pjotr **Sauer** 20**25**, (a Russian affairs reporter for the Guardian),

**[This may be because, [in no small part]{.mark}, [Russia's economic

[Vladimir Putin's entry to power in the early 2000s, accompanied by a

#### Natural gas prevents sanctions and is empirically linked to regime survival

Quentin **Gallea at el.** 20**22**, (PHD from Department of Economics,
University of Lausanne), "Power in the Pipeline", October 10, 2022,
https://arxiv.org/pdf/2210.03572

[This paper provides new results and insights on the resource curse and,
in particular, on the [special role played by natural gas in recent
history]{.mark}. [Increases in the]{.mark} centrality of a country in
the [gas trade network result in a higher probability of regime
survival]{.mark}]{.underline}, and such a leader durability effect does
not appear to be due to greater economic opportunities. Rather, we find
that there is clear evidence that increased centrality of a country
reduces the willingness of other countries in the same network to
sanction the gas-central country. [Put differently, [gas
centrality]{.mark} does not seem to increase the total welfare of the
country but rather [has a significant effect on]{.mark} the
[consolidation of power by the leaders]{.mark}.]{.underline} Could one
read Ukraine's political evolution through the lens of these results?
The country used to have a high betweenness centrality in the gas
market, which however was reduced by the creation of the Georgia-Turkey
pipeline in 2007 and the strengthening of the direct pipeline potential
between Russia and Germany. [According to our results from the global
statistical investigation, [the impact of this relative loss of
centrality would be higher government turnover]{.mark} [and the country
being less shielded from sanctions or other actions by countries in the
same gas network.]{.mark} On another note, our findings also illustrate
how hesitant/reluctant European countries have been to sanction gas
imports from gas-central Russia after its invasion of Ukraine in Spring
2022.]{.underline}

# Misc

### Prolif Good

#### The nuclear threat of extinction is overblown.

Paul **Poast 25** (associate professor in the Department of Political
Science at the University of Chicago, 04/18/2025, "Nuclear Proliferation
Fears May Be Overblown,"
https://www.worldpoliticsreview.com/nuclear-proliferation-trump/?one-time-read-code=3668861753109920162503,
World Politics Review, accessed 7/23/25.) //KG

We are not on the verge of a nuclear free world and may well [never
achieve](https://www.worldpoliticsreview.com/nuclear-weapons-nobel-peace-prize/) that
aim. While this is worrisome enough, there is a new concern: that the
world is on the cusp of heading in the [opposite
direction](https://www.politico.com/news/magazine/2025/04/11/trump-says-he-fears-nuclear-weapons-so-why-is-he-making-them-more-popular-00278790).
Though this week saw the resumption of talks between the United States
and Iran to prevent Iran from developing a nuclear weapon, whether those
talks will succeed is far from certain. After all, it was the first
Trump administration that ended the original Iran nuclear deal, which
had been signed in 2015.

But Iran is not even the primary worry in this brave new world of
nuclear proliferation risk. States that had long been under the U.S.
nuclear umbrella, from Japan to Poland to South Korea and Germany, are
all considering developing their own nuclear weapons. The reelection of
Donald Trump and his administration's insistence that long-standing U.S.
allies do more for their defense while the U.S. does less is leading
these states to question the U.S. commitment to their security. As South
Korea's Foreign Minister Cho Tae-yul remarked recently, Seoul is indeed
considering a nuclear "[Plan
B](https://www.usatoday.com/story/news/world/2025/03/13/trump-concerns-lead-south-korea-to-say-developing-nukes-not-off-table/80837029007/)"
in light of the unpredictability of relying on the U.S. nuclear
deterrent.

Some existing nuclear powers are trying to step into the vacuum and
offer non-nuclear allies reassurance. France and Britain, the other two
NATO allies with nuclear weapons, have offered to replace the U.S.
umbrella in Europe. But from the perspective of states like Germany and
Poland, that may simply replace one unsteady nuclear armed ally with
another. France's Emmanuel Macron
may [offer](https://www.reuters.com/world/europe/frances-macron-address-nation-late-wednesday-2025-03-05/) "protection
of our allies on the European continent through our (nuclear)
deterrence," but would he actually authorize the use of the bomb when
the fallout could feasibly reach French soil? For non-nuclear states,
self-help seems like the safest bet.

The possibility of the number of nuclear states suddenly proliferating
seems to be coming at the worst time. The world was already on edge from
the prospect of seeing nuclear weapons used in conflict for the first
time in 80 years, largely due to Russia's rattling of its nuclear saber.
Whether placing its weapons on [higher
alert](https://www.worldpoliticsreview.com/russia-nuclear-threat-ukraine-war/) or [revising](https://www.worldpoliticsreview.com/russia-nuclear-doctrine-threats/) its
nuclear doctrine to make first use more likely, Russia's actions have
been unnerving the world. Even if Russia doesn't resort to the use of
large, strategic nuclear weapons against the population centers of
Ukraine or a NATO ally, there has been worry that Russia will use a
smaller
"[tactical](https://www.cfr.org/article/if-russia-goes-nuclear-three-scenarios-ukraine-war)"
nuclear weapon on the battlefield against Ukrainian forces. The world
could soon have a host of states who similarly place their nuclear
weapons on a hair trigger.

However, it's also possible that now is the best time to witness a
proliferation of nuclear weapons states. **[[Proliferation may not
change much at all and could even prove
stabilizing.]{.underline}]{.mark}**

If states seek the bomb for their own security, specifically because the
nuclear deterrent they long relied upon is no longer in effect, should
that be a worry? While it's
nearly [impossible](https://www.worldpoliticsreview.com/nuclear-weapons-deterrence/) to
know whether nuclear deterrence will hold**[, it is]{.underline}** also
**[clear]{.underline}** **[that]{.underline}** the
**[states]{.underline}** that are newly developing the bomb are doing so
out of a need to deter aggression, not bolster aggression.

Indeed, this is exactly why the eminent international relations scholar
Kenneth Waltz
famously [argued](https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/05679328108457394) that
[**[nuclear proliferation would actually be a net positive for
humanity]{.underline}**. His logic was grounded in the idea **[that
nuclear weapons are largely defensive weapons]{.underline}**]{.mark}:
given their destructive capacity, states tend only to use them for
punishment purposes as they render the territory on which they are used
uninhabitable. Waltz saw defensive weapons as stabilizing and, hence,
arms races involving defensive weapons should not only be of little
concern, but actually encouraged. The greater the capacity of states to
deter aggression by threatening massive retaliation with nuclear
weapons, the more likely it is that deterrence will hold. This leads to
a bit of a paradox: While an individual nuclear weapon is quite
dangerous, a world full of nuclear weapons is more stable and peaceful
than a world without such weapons. More nuclear weapons should be
welcomed,
not [feared](https://www.foreignaffairs.com/articles/ukraine/2022-06-15/world-power-and-fear),
according to Waltz.

This logic underpinned John
Mearsheimer's [recommendation](https://www.foreignaffairs.com/articles/ukraine/1993-06-01/case-ukrainian-nuclear-deterrent) in
1993 that Ukraine should retain the large quantity of nuclear weapons it
inherited following the collapse of the Soviet Union. While Ukraine did
not immediately possess the ability to launch those weapons, that
capacity could have been developed and was a lower technological hurdle
than creating the weapons themselves. Possessing the warheads would have
given Ukraine a bargaining chip that it subsequently lacked when Russia
began its aggressive incursions against the country in the mid-2010s.

Similarly, consider the 2003 Iraq War. The ostensible rationale for the
war was,
as [argued](https://www.worldpoliticsreview.com/us-iraq-invasion-al-qaeda-saddam-hussein-bush/) by
the administration of then-U.S. President George W Bush, to prevent Iraq
from developing a nuclear weapon, something it had
come [close](https://www.nti.org/analysis/articles/iraq-nuclear/) to
achieving in the early 1990s. If Iraq had already acquired a bomb, would
the U.S. have led an invasion of the country? Of course, the fear was
that Saddam Hussein would be more likely than most leaders to use such a
weapon. But it's also likely he would have recognized that deploying it
would have induced states in the region, such as Israel, or outside the
region, such as the United States, to retaliate in a manner that would
have ended his regime.

Of course, even if all states have nuclear weapons, fear of being the
"second strike" state, and hence incurring the brunt of a mutually
devastating nuclear exchange, could drive states to launch first even if
they have defensive intentions. This is why non-proliferation has been a
foundational component of U.S. foreign policy since it first developed a
nuclear weapon in 1945.

Still, it is possible that nuclear proliferation won't end up being a
bad thing. As
I [wrote](https://www.worldpoliticsreview.com/fears-of-a-nuclear-iran-jcpoa-or-not-might-be-overblown/) previously
**[with respect to Iran's pursuit of a nuclear weapon, "[It might be
better to let them have it and then learn to live with
it]{.mark}]{.underline}**." While this might sound like a plea to accept
a bad outcome, it's possible that wars between great powers and even
wars by great powers against others are less likely in a world without
nuclear weapons. Though Nobel Peace prizes don't tend to be awarded to
those who encourage nuclear proliferation, **[[it's quite feasible
that]{.mark} Donald [Trump]{.mark} [may]{.mark}, in an unintentional and
ironic way, [make the world more peaceful by pursuing policies that lead
more countries to acquire]{.mark} the [bomb]{.mark}.]{.underline}**

### Not past tipping points

#### We are not yet past tipping points

Andrei **Ionescu** 4-28-20**25** (PhD in cognitive humanities from the
University of Padua (Italy), and conducted extensive interdisciplinary
research at the intersection of the sciences and the humanities at
Constructor University, the University of Groningen, Cardiff University,
and the University of Bucharest, multiple times published.), "Climate
tipping points: How close are we to irreversible change?", Earth.com,
https://www.earth.com/news/climate-tipping-points-how-close-are-we-to-irreversible-change/

[The [planet is edging]{.mark} far [closer]{.mark} to irreversible
change [than]{.mark} most [policy debate]{.mark}s [acknowledge]{.mark},
according to the most comprehensive probability analysis yet of climate
"tipping points."]{.underline} The research was a collaboration between
the University of Exeter's Global Systems Institute and the University
of Hamburg. [The experts have calculated that, [if governments follow
their present course]{.mark}, [there is]{.mark} roughly a [two-in-three
likelihood that]{.mark} at least [one major]{.mark} Earth-system
[component will tip into a]{.mark} radically different and largely
[irreversible state]{.mark}.]{.underline} Tipping points measured in the
study Tipping points are thresholds where small incremental warming
forces a system -- such as an ice sheet, an ocean circulation pattern,
or a vast forest -- into a self-propagating transition. To gauge just
how close humanity is to those thresholds, lead author Jakob Deutloff
and colleagues looked at sixteen well-studied tipping elements. Their
list included collapse of the West Antarctic and Greenland ice sheets,
disruption of the Atlantic Meridional Overturning Circulation, wholesale
die-off of the Amazon rainforest, abrupt thaw of Arctic permafrost, and
several others ranging from coral reefs to boreal forests. Using the
Finite-amplitude Impulse Response (FaIR) climate model coupled to a
simplified representation of Amazon dieback and permafrost thaw, the
team ran tens of thousands of Monte-Carlo simulations. These runs
sampled three layers of uncertainty: the planet's sensitivity to
greenhouse gases, the temperature thresholds at which each element could
tip, and the time each system would take to complete its transition. Are
the tipping points avoidable? [The researchers then drove the model with
five standard emissions pathways, known as Shared Socioeconomic Pathways
(SSPs). The [scenario that best matches today's national
policies]{.mark} -- SSP2-4.5, which delivers about 2.8 °C of warming by
2100 -- [proved alarmingly unsafe]{.mark}.]{.underline} Averaged across
all sixteen tipping elements, the probability of at least one being
triggered reached 62 percent. Nine of the elements exceeded 50 percent
likelihood individually, including the West Antarctic Ice Sheet, the
Greenland Ice Sheet and the Amazon rainforest. **[[Under higher-emission
futures]{.mark} such as SSP3-7.0 or SSP5-8.5, [tipping virtually becomes
unavoidable]{.mark} for most elements. [Conversely,]{.mark} [more
aggressive mitigation plans]{.mark} that align [with the Paris
Agreement]{.mark} -- SSP1-2.6 (about 1.8 °C peak warming) and SSP1-1.9
(1.5 °C) -- [cut the average probability to]{.mark} roughly 37 percent
and [14]{.mark} percent, respectively, though they do not eliminate risk
entirely.]{.underline}** Extra push from carbon-cycle feedbacks [The
study also tackled a longstanding concern that emissions released by one
tipping element -- specifically [Amazon dieback or permafrost
thaw]{.mark} --]{.underline} could generate sufficient extra warming to
push others over the edge. By embedding a conceptual carbon-feedback
module inside FaIR, the researchers found that even under current-policy
warming, [additional carbon from those two sources would [raise]{.mark}
global [temperature by]{.mark} a median of only [0.22]{.mark} °C by
2300. That figure is an [order of magnitude smaller than the warming
driven directly by human activity]{.mark} and increases the average
tipping probability by just three percentage points.]{.underline} In
high-emission futures the absolute carbon release is larger, but the
boost to global temperature is proportionally smaller because
anthropogenic emissions dominate. The conclusion: **[[feedbacks]{.mark}
[from these two]{.mark} carbon-rich systems [are not]{.mark}, on their
own, [likely to set off a domino cascade]{.mark}]{.underline}**.
Nonetheless, the authors caution that other physical interactions --
such as changes in ocean circulation or cloud cover -- could still
amplify risks in ways not captured by the present model. Global warming:
A question of timing One of the biggest unknowns remains how fast each
tipping element responds once its threshold temperature is crossed. The
team calculated two extremes. In an "instantaneous" view, an element
flips as soon as the global mean temperature passes its threshold. In an
"equilibrium" view, tipping occurs only if temperature remains above
that threshold after the climate stabilizes. For high-emission
scenarios, where warming climbs continuously, the distinction hardly
matters. In mitigation pathways that involve a mid-century overshoot
followed by cooling, however, the assumed reaction speed makes a
dramatic difference: instantaneous triggering can more than double the
probability relative to the equilibrium calculation. Preventing the
climate tipping points ["The [good news from our study is]{.mark} that
the [power to prevent]{.mark} climate tipping points [is still in our
hands]{.mark}," Deutloff said. "By [moving towards]{.mark} a more
sustainable future with [lower emissions]{.mark}, [the risk of]{.mark}
triggering these [tipping points is significantly reduced]{.mark}. And
it appears that breaching tipping points within the Amazon and the
permafrost region should not necessarily trigger others."]{.underline}
Study co-author Tim Lenton warmer that climate tipping points could have
devastating consequences for humanity,. "It is clear that we are
currently on a dangerous trajectory -- with tipping points likely to be
triggered unless we change course rapidly." ["[We need urgent]{.mark}
global action -- including the triggering of '[positive tipping
points']{.mark} in our societies and economies -- to reach a safe and
sustainable future," said Lenton. Rapid transitions are urgently needed
Positive tipping points refer to [self-reinforcing social and
technological shifts]{.mark} -- [such as]{.mark} rapid [adoption of
renewable energy]{.mark}, electric vehicles or new food systems -- that
can drive emissions downward at exponential rates.]{.underline} The
authors argue that deliberately engineering such rapid transitions is
now humanity's best chance of keeping global warming below levels where
probabilities of catastrophic change climb toward certainty.

Michael **McLaughlin** 20**25**, (Senior Counterintelligence Advisor for

The Cybersecurity and Infrastructure Security Agency must refocus its
